method of Nature, the other a method of Art. The first teaches the simple and
pure names of natural things: the second concerns the root and ground of
the tongues; and considers principally the nownes and verbs, and the natural
pronounciation, according to the equity of Grammar. For in these three
consists the profoundest mystery of all tongues; because they aid much the
judgment, the invention and memory, to read, to understand, to write.

Will you propound and begin with this way of teaching at the first day of the
young scholars beginning?

No, no, we must first lay a foundation concerning the root of nownes and
verbes, and that he have gone a little forward in the tongue that he studieth,
before this method be presented to him; then after may we teach the scholar to
translate Dialogues, Commedies and Prose, and set abroch this method of Nature
and Art, which shall engender the practice and habit of swift speaking, and
perfect understanding of any thing both in speaking, reading and vvriting.

Show me, I pray you, one familiar example, to the end to satisfy me more
amply herein.

Here are two: I will begin with one of God, who is the Prince and King of
Nature, and I will end with another of the Devil, the enemy of God and Nature.

The Method of Nature.
GOd the Father.

Jesus Christ the son.

The Holy Ghost.

The Deity.

The Trinity.

Almighty.

To Create.

The Heaven.

The Firmament.

The World.

A Star.

A Sphere.

The Throne of glory.

A Legion of Angels.

An Angel.

A feigned God.

Lucifer.

A Goddess.

An Idol.

An Idolater.

The earth.

Our Savior.

Our Redeemer.

An only Son

Blessed.

The people of God, beloved.

To Crucify.

To murder.

A Jew.

An Infidel.

The Praxis of Art.
GOd the Father almighty with his Son Jesus Christ, and the Holy Ghost, to
wit, the Deity, or the holy Trinity, has created the Heaven, the Firmament,
the Stars, and other celestial Globes, called Spheres, for his habitation:
and being environed in his Throne of glory with legions of Angels, has
confounded all the feigned Gods and Goddesses, of the Earth, will have no
Idols nor Idolaters, has chased the Angels which sinned* Lucifer and his
companions out of Heaven, and has sent Jesus Christ, his only Son our
blessed Savior and Redeemer, to be for the sins of the world crucified and
murdered of the Jews, once the well-beloved people of God, now an Infidel
nation, cruel and vagrant in all the corners of the world.

This is the first example, the second follows in the next page.

The Method of Nature.
AN Angel.

A Devil.

A malign Spirit.

The enemy of God.

The justice divine.

Hell.

A deep pit.

Lucifer.

Beelzebub.

Astaroth.

The Prince of Devils.

A spirit.

One possessed with a Devil.

A little Devil.

A she Devil.

Punishment.

Sin.

To fall.

To rack.

To lay in chains.

To torment.

To burn.

Fire eternal.

Unquenchable fire.

To smoke.

To blaze.

Brimstone.

Pitch.

A damned soul.

The pains of Hell.

Dark.

Stinking.

Dark as Hell.

To beat one another.

To strike one another

To weep.

To gnash the teeth.

To tempt.

To curse.

To cast firebrands.

The Praxis of Art.
THe Angels sinning became Devils and wicked spirits, enemies to God and men,
and by the divine justice being cast down from their glory on high, fell to
the deepest pit of Hell. Where God had prepared a place of punishment according
to their sin, and has set Lucifer, Beelzebub and Astaroth, wicked spirits,
princes reigning over the other little Devils, tortured, tormented and chained
all together in eternal and unquenchable fire. Whether also go down all the
Souls tempted to evil without repentance, and are damned in the same pains
of Hell, being a place full of Brimstone and Pitch, very dark, stinking,
obscure, smoking, and always burning: where they shall be for ever beating and
striking one another, weeping and gnashing their teeth, and flinging flaming
firebrands one at an other, without any hope of ever departing from that place,
for in Hell there is no redemption, according to the holy Scriptures.

There is the second Example, What think you thereof?

Me thinks that these examples are rather transnaturall then natural.

By your leave no: for although that God and the Devil be Metaphisicall in
their proper essences: the other things subject and incident as it were to
them, are verily natural.

I understand you very well now.

Very hardly, I think.

Indeed I do: you say that there is a method to further and profit more
speedily those that study the tongues, contrived by Nature and Art: Nature,
you say, touches principally the natural order, the place, and the sympathy
of Nownes and Verbes, or of other parts of speech joined together, as they are
proper and fit for one and the same matter:

Art concerns the judgment of the Grammar rules, for the use of scholars,
when they shall need, either in composing or in speaking.

You say well now.

Is this a pedagogicall or scholasticall exercise.

It is both.

Is this the most assured means to attain the tongues?

I have obscrued it of men of great spirit, that it is far the most
expedientest: moreover the learned know how hard a thing the two methods well
contrived, that they call Eruditionis modus, or (...) is to be conceived, and
that Aristotle himself, who has so excellently taught it, and so learnedly
showed it in this his wonderful work, called the Organo^, is understood of
very few.

As far as I see then, when we have all done it shall be but some imperfect
work, of the order and way that we have, to understand more languages then
our own mother tongue.

You say true: and to confess the truth unto you, we must now spend a man's
whole lifetime to learn but the bare words of Tongues, be it of the Hebrew,
Greek, or Latin: and content our selves to steal a little out of them: to
wit, to learn to babble: and in steed of being excellent well seen in
Philosophy divine and human, we must hunt after sillables, words, periods,
and some other such little exercises, which make our chinnes and heads look
gray, and even make an end of our days before we have indeed known or tasted
what Law, Physic, and Divinity are, which are the principal sciences.

Sir pardon me I pray you, for I trouble you here from your studies.

You do not truly, you are very welcome to me.

I will take my leave of you now sir, and come see you again after dinner.

You shall be very welcome, when it shall please you to return.

God be with you sir.

A good and long life sir.

Dialogue 2. Of the dignity of Orators, and excellency of tongues.
GOd save and keep you sir.

Welcome, welcome truly sir.

I come to visit you this afternoon, according to my promise.

Now am I at good leisure to speak with you.

We have discoursed this morning of method, and the way to learn strange
languages.

What shall we now do?

What you will, sir.

I have great desire to learn the tongues, but I know not which are the most
famous and renowned tongues in the world, at this present day.

I will reckon them to you: the Hebrew, Syrian, Arabian, Greek, the Latin,
Italian, Spanish, and the French. There are eight of the best and noblest
tongues that I can name you, but there are infinite other.

O if it were Gods will that I could but only speak these eight: but it is not
possible for a man to possess them all at once.

What say you man? there is a man in the Emperors Court who speaks them all
naturally: and speaks besides many others most eloquently.

I cannot believe it.

It is true, the man is yet living.

How call you him?

He is called Scaliger: and to confirm that which I say, du Bartas a most
excellent French Poet, has made mention of him in his Second Week in the day
which is called Babilone.

He wrote in French, I understand him not.

Because he is a man worthy of faith, I will hasard to translate his
testimony: albeit I am not ignorant how great my sawcines shall be by
presuming to take upon me to counterfeit by our English his inimitable style.

These are his verses: These gifts in only man infused
from God we must impute,
Of Fortitude, of Equity, of Honor
to dispute,
Of wisdom, temperance, God and
heaven, of water, air, and ground,
In him who has a filed phrase, and
choice of terms y-found:
Not by one only Idiome
his secrets to unfold,
But as the learned Scaliger,
whom men the wonder hold,
Of this our age the muses star,
whose tongue is not to seek,
Roling the Hebrew, Latin, Dutch
English, Castilian, Greek.
French-fine, Italian-glorious,
Numidian, Arabesko,
Assyrian, Caldaean, Persian tongues,
the Turkish and Moresko.
O rich and supple spirit that can
his tongue so quickly change,
Cameleon-like into what author
likes him best to range.
A worthy son of great Iulus,
worthy fellow mate,
Of Syluius his first brother-borne


Whom Gascons celebrate.

There is a worthy praise surely for him*

I am assured that he deserved it well.

The judgment be referred to all those who know him well: for he is known all
over Europe, to be a man of an admirable wit.

Which do yoo deem to have been the first speech in the world?

Without doubt it is the Hebrew, which is the most ancient, and the very same
that our father Adam spoke, being in Paradise with God.

The Hebrew, is it mysterious, as the Egyptian tongue, which is Hierogliphicke
and Caballisticke.

In few words it contains many things, is maruellously significatiue,
energicall, pure, chaste, holy and divine, manifests clearly and breefely all
that any man can think in his heart, and when need requires to express the
least hidden secrets and deepest corners of the heart, it leaves nothing
unspoken, having a gravity, sweetness, a marvelous viuacitie and efficacy
in every word, period and discourse; more admirable then all the sweetness of
the Greek, which with his words of one signification, his Epithetons rashly
devised or curiously sought out, his subtle translations, his made words, his
Aorists, and other fine devises, is no more comparable to the other, then the
chattering of a Iay to the sweet note of the Nightingale.

Who is the best Hebrew Author?

It is God himself: for the Eternal God giving and vvriting himself twice
his law to his people, and speaking with his mouth in the mountain to Moses,
and to other of his servants, used the Hebrew tongue. The Angels, and the
Prophets have done the same.

What is the cause that the Hebrew is at this day in so small account?

The impiety and the contempt of the true Divinity, is the cause that the
Hebrew is not esteemed of, but it is so much the more cherished of those that
know what it is.

The Hebrew then is the first and most ancient of all tongues?

It has the first and most ancient place among all tongues that have been,
that are, and which shall be in the world.

There are some for all that which rashly say to the contrary, and a certain
Goropius a Physician of Brabant, in his books entitled Origines Antuerpianae,
has been so bold to write to prove his Flemish to be the most ancient and
first borne of tongues.

Tush, tush, sir, the honest gentleman mistakes himself in his gross volumes.

For all that he labors to prove it expressly.

O these are Flemish flamflues, the poor man has lost his labor therein.

There are more besides him who have written as much as he.

I believe as well the one as the other.

There Fevers quartains. We shall show them that their flambumbarkin is not of
the antiquity that they pretend, but was ingendered in Babel, and to be
nothing but a barbarous hibber-Iybber, corrupted, effeminat and variable in
comparison of the Hebrew, whatsoever they dare spit to the contrary.

It is then a very great impudency in them to write such filthy lies.

If the Commicall Aristophanes were alive, he should have here a good argument
to write a Commedie of their impudency.

The Assyrian tongue what language is it?

It is a speech so derived from the Hebrew, that they have great affinity
together.

Which is the best author?

Jesus Christ spoke the Syriac, and the most part of the new Testament is
written in it: nevertheless it is in small account at this day, only they
speak it about Jerusalem, at Tripoly in Syria, and in the confines, no where
else. It was the tongue that was spoken under the Assyrian Monarchy. And in it
is the Thalmud of the Jews written.

Have you never studied the Arabian tongue?

No sir, but as men say, it is a goodly speech, rich, fluent, and which has
more scope than any other that is spoken in the woorld.

We find very few books written in the Arabian.

There are many manuscripts, but very few printed yet: when I was at Rome, it
is now at the least seven or eight years since, that Pope Gregory the
thirteenth began to set up a Print there in Campo de Fiori, and sought to
recover many manuscripts in the Arabian and Syrian tongues, caused very fair
Caracters to be cut at his charges, and sent all abroad to get workmen in
that mystery, to set them a work thereupon. I cannot tell whether the
Popes that have been created since his time have prosecuted this good beginning.

I doubt it much. For otherwise some of the books would have been come to our
hands. But I pray you in what country does the Arabian most besteed us?

Throughout all the Leuant countries. It is the best tongue in the world to
travail over all the great Turks kingdoms, over greatest part of Asia, through
all the countries of the Iapanians, through China, through the empire of
Presbiter John, and through all Affrike, and by it a man passes well over the
third part of the world. It is a singular fine tongue surely.

I think that it is the daughter of the Hebrew.

It is come of the Hebrew without doubt.

All the Turks and Mahumetains speak Arabian, because that Mahumet their
false Prophet, wrote his law or Alcoran in the same.

What people are these Arabians?

The Arabians at this present are almost all thieves and robbers, who dwell
but little in closed cities, but roue abroad spoiling and robbing trauaillers,
who make their voiages through the deserts and forests of Arabia.

Truly the travelers say so.

Notwithstanding there are amongst them very many who are natural and skillful
Physicians.

Of whom are the most eloquent vvritings that we have from the Arabians.

Those are the works of Auerroes the Commentator, a most excellent
Philosopher, who has written most singular Commentaries on the greatest part
of Aristotle's works.

But we understand them not in the Arabian.

They are translated into Latin and printed at Venice.

This Philosopher Auerroes was a man of a subtle spirit, and of a most
profound judgment.

What other good author have we among the Arabians?

There is besides him Auicenna, an eloquent orator in his Arabian tongue; and
many others, of whom we have the writings in many places of Europe.

He was a Physician.

So also was the other.

After these three sisters, which esteem you the best tongue?

The ancient Greek; but as for the vulgar Greek at this day it is very
gross.

Where shall we find the purest, and that which is good indeed?

We must fetch it from the fountains of these great Greek Orators, Poets and
Rhetoricians: out of Plato Xenopho^, Demosthenes, Isocrates, Homere, Euripides,
Sophocles, Pindarus, Plutarke, Basil, Chrysostom, Nazianzene, and out of
others in great number.

Who was the most ancient Greek author that we have?

It is Homer in his Iliads and Odyssees written in 48. books.

Homer, was he the first Greek Poet or not?

I think so, yet I cannot tell whether Amphion, Orpheus, Lynus, and some other
were before him or no: but I am sure that this great Greek Poet, although he
were blind from his infancy, has nevertheless pend things so profound and
admirable, that some have dared to write of him, that if all the wisdom of
Poets were moulte^ together in a great furnace, it could not compare with
something so surpassing which shines in his works, nor himself if he were
raised from the dead, could not do that again which he has brought to pass.

I believe there was never Greek nor Latin that was able to imitate or
counterfeit his style.

Truly his wit was admirable, his inventions inimitable, his discourses
natural, his verses flowing, full of art, and which have infinite graces the
more we consider them: beside a hidden sense in them, and the fountain of all
humain sciences springing out of them, as we see a thousand and a thousand
sundry pieces of his poesies fly into the books of Philosophers, Geographers
Orators, and Historiographers. Witness be Plutarke hereof, amongst all the
rest.

Plato, was not be an Author as much renowned in Greece as he?

Plato the most famous Philosopher (no less honored in Greece the^ that
Homer, for whose bones seven cities of Greece warred so long one against
another) was surnamed, the Divine, a spirit maruellously pure and profound,
lofty in all his discourses: a true disciple of him who making profession to
know but one thing, (that he knew Nothing) well showed that he knew all that
may be learned in the world concerning the world.

They say that he had seen a piece of the old Testament.

I cannot tell. He may have seen some of the Prophets: to be short, touching
the knowledge of salvation, he and his master Socrates, were altogether
ignorant thereof.

Who has had the third place among the Greek Orators?

Herodotus the Ionian, who was a grave, sage and most eloquent
Historiographer in his Ionian Idiome.

What say you of Demosthenes, where put you him?

He shall have his place here, as he has deserved it. Demosthenes indeed is
the Prince of Greek Orators, the rule of all those who will speak
learnedly, a man who handles the hearts of his hearers as he lists,
excelling in all his discourses, which are partly in light, and read with
great fruit, and of great use for those that can tell how to help themselves
with the^.

Who are the ornaments and pillars of the Latin tongue.

The principal is Cicero, surnamed the Father of Eloquence.

I vvonder wherefore he was so extremely hated of Catiline and Mark Antony.

It was because he would not abide nor favor their trecheries and
conspiracies against the commonwealth of the Romans: but he pursued them both
lustily, as his Orations called his Inuectiues against Catiline and his
Philippicks do well witness.

O the brave man that this was! it was great pity truly he was not a
Christian.

Erasmus doubts not but that he may be saved: he has spoken so divinely of
God: I remember where he says, Si vnus Deus est vt aiunt, in his book of
Old Age.

What would he have said if he had been a Christian, saying so much being but
a poor Pagan?

O my God! I am ravished considering the quickness and viuacitie of his spirit.

His works so many times printed, so many times read over and over again of
all, the vvritings of so many learned men, who have borrowed all their fine
discourses out of this flood of Eloquence, from this rich spirit learned and
eloquent past all men's vvonder, do verify that which a Christian Poet does
say of him: Raging Antonius mortal foe,
a scourge of Catiline,
From whose sweet breast does stream
a flood of Eloquence divine,
A flood whose current seems to cast
forth lightening and thunder.
Which ravish all the rarest spirits
of men, and make them wunder.


Who has vvoon the price next after Cicero for the Latin?

It has been Julius Caesar, who has been valiant among the valiant, and
eloquent among the eloquent, as his life written by Plutarke, and his
Commentaries de Bello Gallico, where he has taken the pen out of the hands of
all learned men, and discouraged them from vvriting of Histories, seeing they
cannot once come near his perfection by far, do prove it sufficiently.

Who shall be put in the third room?

That shall Salust the Historian, of whom we have two short histories, but
sententious, full of good matter and testimonies of that ancient vigor of the
Romans.

Who is cheifest among the Latin Poets?

Virgill is the Father of the Latin Poets, of whom we cannot speak too
well: his Georgicks and Aeneidos being books admirable above all other books
of humanity do make sufficient proof.

They say that his verses are rugged and hard.

I speak not of the excellency of his verses, but his judgment, the
profoundnesse of his inventions, his Decorum, his modesty, his gravity, his
majesty, and his conceits, finer then any other appear not only in every
book, but in every one of his verses, where one shall find a thousand and a
thousand fine secrets of all sciences, of which we have in him as it were an
Epitome, besides he has his words so proper, his epithetons so fit, his
metaphors and figures bestowed so well in their places, and every where his
language in so pure and eloquent terms, without any affection or other fault
at all.

Who has been the most excellent Orator in the Italian tongue?

John Boccace, who wrote now a good while ago, but very finely and purely, as
his Decameron, his Fiammetta, his Philocopo, his Labyrintho, and other of his
books loved by the children of this world do well witness.

Who are the best Tuscan or Florentine Poets?

These three which I will now name:

In the first place shall be the fine Poet Francis Petrarke, who wrote his
Poesies since the time of John Boccace, and has invented many trim words, and
enriched his verses with infinite pretty devises taken out of other Authors,
and many devised of his own wit also, be^^de he was very bold and has some
sonnets, chapters, and Cantoes altogether admirable.

What other Italian Poets will you name me?

Two only, the one is Lodouike Ariosto of Ferrara, who has published a Poem
called Mad-Rowland, in verses so sweet and full of delight, that all Italy
has them still in their mouth, full of passions in his discourses, and very
pleasant by reason of the diversity of things that he recites, his fables
being so cunningly disguised, that a man is moved sometimes in reading of
them, as if they were things truly come to pass, or at the least having some
resemblance of troth.

Who is the other?

Torquato Tasso, a fine scholar truly, who is yet living, the last Italian
Poet who is of any great fame in our age, but vvorthie of the first honor;
besides that he is a divine Poet, he is also a most eloquent Orator and
Rhetoricyan, as his missiue Epistles do show very well. This Youth fell mad
for the love of an Italian less descended of a great house, when I was in
Italy.

What other fine books has he made?

Many: there are three Toomes of his works printed at Ferrara, vvherin there
are diverse sorts of verses of all kinds of fine inventions: a Commedie, a
Tragedy, diverse Dialogues and discourses in Prose, all vvorthie the reading of
the vvisest and quickest spirits of Europe.

Is that all that he has written?

No, for he has the pen in hand every day.

You have forgotten his Gierusalemme liberata.

You say true, this child has written in Heroical verses one excellent Poem
amongst all other Italian Poesies, entitled as you say, wherein all the
riches of the Greeks and Latins are gathered together and enchaced so
cunningly past all others skill, with such grace, brevity, gravity,
learning, liveliness, and viuacitie that is remarqued to have been in Virgill
the Prince of Latin Poets.

Who have been the quickest Spanish wits of any fame in the world?

For an Historian Antony Gueuare, who was Secretary to the Emperor Carolus
quintus.

I have read over and over again almost all his works: but who are the best
Spanish Poets?

They are Boscan, Grenade, Garcilasso and Mont-maior.

I vvonder that men get them not translated into English.

They would have no grace.

Why so? we find them almost all translated into Latin Italian and French.

I believe it well, yet have they more grace in their Castillian, which is
the purest Spanish dialect, in which the learned write and speak ordinarily.

Amongst the French, who are the most eloquent authors?

There are very many who have written both in rithme and prose, but I will
name you only one half dosen of brave men, three Poets; and three Orators,
and Rhetoricians whose likes are not to be found:

First you have Clement Marot, that was King Francis Poet, who was admirable
for his time.

He was the Kings fool, for since his time they call all fools and idiots in
France Marots, You are a Marot, say they, A vengeance of the Marot.

You deceive your self greatly, he was the Poet Royal, not a fool royal:
of whom the fine poesies shall be read and read again, as long as Yea and Nay
shall last amongst learned men.

You will pardon me sir, for I speak no otherwise then a fool by hear say
and at all adventure.

They say that he transported the Muses beyond the mountains, and attyred them
on the French fashion. He has done marvelous well in his beginnings truly.

Hold him of my word for a natural Poet: when you shall understand French
perfectly read me his books, if you have the leisure.

Who is the second French Poet in your opinion?

It is Peter Ronsard, rich in the spoils of the Greeks and Latins; as his
Loves, his diverse Poesies, his Odes, Elegies and Hymns do testify, in the
which we read all kind of verses, all kind of Arguments in all kind of styles.

Who shall make the third?

We will put down for the third the Christian Poet William Sallustius, Lord
of Bartas, who has written more in Three-Weekes, then all other French Poets,
or all other Poets either Pagan or Christian have done in all their life.
Besides a man of as great a wit as ever France bred any.

Who are the most singular French Rhetoricians?

We have James Amiot, who translated out of Greek into French the Ethiopian
History of Heliodorus, the seven books of the History of Diodorus the
Sicilian, and all the works of Plutarke, wherein he has happily spent his
time.

What style writes he?

High and lofty: his language is pure and simple, not affected, not coined: to
be brief, he shows himself a right Frenchman.

What other French Orators will you name me?

France has nursed many sweet men of great fame, who have drawn their
eloquence from Rome, from Greece from Egypt, and from all parts and corners of
the world; amongst an infinite number I will speak but only of two
Rhetoricians, who have got the goal from all the other: the one is this
Blaisius Vigenerus, so famous and renowned in France, who has also translated
many books, as the History of Polonia, a piece of Titus Liuius, Caesar,
Philostratus, and Chalcondilus.

Truly he has been virtuously occupied.

This Blaisius Vigenerus has much fine skill in his writings, and many choice
and fine carriages. It is said that among all the puples of the Muses that
France has suckled, this Vigenere has said so well, that he has shut the
door to all those who shall come after him as well in eloquence of speech as
in learning,

Yet we lack a third man to make an end of this our discourse of Tongues and
Orators.

The honor appertains to Philip de Mornay, who has so terribly combatted
and beaten down the Atheism of our age with lively reasons, arguments,
inductions, and invincible proofs joined with a language twixt grave and
sweet, well compact, thundering with so fine and fit French phrases, that he
brings his Reader whether he lists to have him; and this learned man shall
end our dispute of tongues.

We have to day discoursed lustily of Languages, of Orators, and Rhetoricians.

There are yet in the world so many other tongues, and so many famous Orators,
that a great volume would not suffice to set them all down in order.

Truly the glory and majesty of man appears in nothing more then in his
speech.

It is true, because that thereby he shows his reason, the light of his soul
and body.

In what space think you that a man of a good wit may attain to speak these
eight languages above named?

In eight years it is possible, if he will be studious, and that he have good
direction to speak these eight eloque^tly, with the grace of the true accent,
and to write them swiftly and volubly.

Among these tongues is there not some which are far more noble and glorious
then other some?

It is not to be doubted but the Hebrew of the ancient Fathers Prophets and
Patriarchs, has had the most magnificent accent, and a souueraigne grace in
the pronounciation above all other tongues, although the Jews living at this
present day in the world, speak it harshly, I cannot tell how; but the true
Toscaine, Roman or Florentine tongue, when it is accented aright, and well
pronounced, has a better grace and glory, is more fluent and glib then all
other speeches spoken in Europe.

I am of opinion truly that there is no tongue in the world more heroical
then that.

I will not be of your mind therein.

Why so?

Have you never in your life heard a Mexican; a Moscouite, a Tartare; a
Iapanois or one of China speak his language?

Never in my life.

In the year eightie-foure or five Xistus quintus being Pope of Rome; there
were four young princes or kings, or sons of the kings of Iapan, which came
from these countries of the Orient to Rome with the Iesuist Mesquite an Italian
who had converted them, and brought them thither to do homage and to take the
oath of obedience to the Pope. I heard them speak in passing along* through the
streets: their words are almost all of one syllable, their speech princely,
thundering, proud, glorious, and marvelous lofty.

How many languages speak they in Mexico the great City, so much renowned in
the West Indies.

They speak (as it is reported) four diverse tongues, and these four Mexican
tongues are all so flowing, full of grace and eloquence, that the Greek, the
Latin, the Italian, the Spanish, and the French seem some effeminate notes or
barbarous prittle-prattle in comparison of their loftiness, profoundnesse, and
sweet phrase of speech.

What will you then say of these more Northren tongues of the Moscouites and
Tartarians?

I give them the price and the honor truly, for the Russian and Tartarian are
tongues imperial, thundering, resounding, rattling, noble and generous above
all other tongues on the face of Asie, Europe, and Affrike.

The high Almand retains some little accent of their excellent tone in their
speech: to conclude then, all the Antarticke tongues of the East or West
Indians and those of the other part of the world; have far greater luster,
magnificence and generousnesse in them, then those that we speak in this
other moitie of the Earth.

True it is sir that I am not desirous to learn any of these tongues: also I
know that you do not teach them; but I have a great affection to the French the
which I would desire to learn of you, if it would please you to give me
instruction therein.

I will do for you all that I can possibly.

But I must know how much you take by the week, by the month, by the quarter,
by the year.

I make no merchandising with those whom I teach, for the gifts of the graces
and the noble and virtuous qualities ought not to be set on sale.

Give me what you will, half a crown, if you will, a crown, an angel,
twenty shillings, five pounds, ten pounds, twenty marks, twenty pounds
sterling, by the week, by the month, by the year.

Others teach for fifty and two shillings by the year, that is twelue-pence a
week, which I will give you willingly to be naturally instructed in the French
tongue.

I care not a whit what other mercenaries do; but as for me I will not stand
to bargain in this matter.

Put the case you were a mercenarie Pedante your self; how much would you
ask for the son of a Mecanicke?

If he were the son of a Merchant, he should pay for a Merchant, I would
demand and would take no less then five pounds by the year.

And for the son of a Gentleman?

If he were come af a good house, he should pay me ten pounds a year.

And if he were the son of a great Lord?

By my fe he should pay for a great Lord, and should give me every year
twenty, thirty, or forty pounds at the lest.

You should have very few scholars at that price, for you are to dear.

So also would not I sell noble quallities for the wages of a villain, at a
vile and common price, but every one should pay me according to his estate: the
merchant for a merchant, the gentleman for a gentleman, and a lord for a lord,
according to his degree of honor.

It shall not displease you sir, if I demand where you have learned the
French?

At Paris in France, where I was brought up three years together in the
college of Montagu. I have also been scholemaister at Orleans in the College of
Affricans; and lived ten months at Lions.

Sir you shall excuse me if it please you, it is time that I go to my scholars.

I will advise my self then between this and to morrow, and then we will
come to agreement, if it please God.

God be with you.

Fare you well my good sinnior.

Dialogue 3.
Master Noe-Ionas and Ely, I am very glad to see you in good health!

I thank you my good friend.

How do you?

Very well, at your command and service.

And I at yours truly.

How long is it since we were together at Paris?

It Is at the least seven or eight years.

It Is not so much in my opinion.

I am deceived else, for it was at the first when the duke of Guise did levy
his arms for those of the League.

I vvoondered much of your departure which was so sudden from Paris.

What said they I pray you among my friends and acquaintance?

By my faith they said that you were become a moonke of the order of S. Benet.

O poor man, I was very sorry for you.

True it is after the year of my probationship, I left my habit behind.

You have not cast your frocke into the nettles?

In good faith no: I departed out of the abbie with license, like an honest
man.

You made the three vows of chastity, poverty and obedience.

About some no:

I did not, I tell you, for I was received only but for a young Monk, or as
you say in English, for a Nouice.

But I pray you where have you been since that time?

All abroad, in Italy, in France, in Spain.

Do you not mock?

No truly, why should I mock.

From Paris what way took you?

From Paris I went into Italy.

Have you dwelt at Rome?

I made some abode there?

What news will you tell me out of those quarters?

There are within these 7. years last, four or five popes dead.

Have you never been further then Rome?

Yes, as far as Naples.

How did you escape falling into the Inquisitors hands?

I attired my self like a Pilgrim.

O the fine pilgrim that here is!

How escaped you the inquisition? tell me I pray you.

There is no danger for one who is papal and of the number of Catholics.

How shall he do then who is a Protestant to pass through Italy as far as
Rome.

He must dissemble.

I deem that, he need not.

What then?

He must only carry a good face, for pass once Venice, the inquisition is
there every where.

Have you lived at Venice, Padua, Pauia, Milan, Mantua, Cremona, Pisa, Sienna,
Vrbino?

No, I have passed by them only.

Have you seen Florence, Ferrara and Bolonia?

I have seen and visited them.

Have you past by Lions?

Signor si, upon the bridges and on the pauements.

Came you away by Orleans?

Yea, sir.

Have you trafficked at Paris?

I, in eating and drinking.

Have you not haunted at Roan?

I was never there.

Have you soiourned at Amiens?

Some four or five days.

Seeing that you have seen so many fine cities in these two countries; tell
us also something of Spain.

In Spain the chief cities are Siuillia, the vvarehouse of the Indias, for
the diversity of mer^handise that men bring thither.

There is beside Saragossa, Grenada, Valledolida, Medina del campo, Pampelona
in Navarre, and Lisbona in Portugal: where is also Conymbria, a famous
university.

What other fair Cities are there in Spain?

There are so many, that I cannot name them all.

How many Universities have they there?

There are many: I will name only Salamanca, an University where they study
the Law, Physic, and the seven liberal Sciences.

Where keeps king Philip his court?

For the most part at Madril, or at the Esquireal, where he has built the
most magnificent palace of all Europe, being the dwelling house of the
Catholic king, and it is the fairest building that I ever saw in my life.

In your mind then, it is the most goodly, stateliest and sumptuous building
that a man can imagine.

It Is a palace enriched with great gardens, closes, and orchards, and with the
rarest fruits that a man can wish.

How is it situated?

Upon a pleasant river.

Of what form is this building?

It is built six corner fashion: and the pillars stand diameter wise.

By your reckoning the Palace of the Pope, of the Cardinal Medicis, and many
other in Rome are nothing comparable to this.

It Is a hundred times more magnificent then those, or any other in Italy, name
as many as you will.

Surely this palace is a terrestrial paradise, such as promises Mahomet in
his Alcoran to his Musselmans.

O my friend, the king of Spain has more then twenty ships which come home
every year from the ysles of Perles, from Peru, from Mexico and the Canibals,
laden with lingots of gold and silver, pearls and stones, and raw silk in
great quantity.

I vvoonder why he has not then paued his palace ten mile round with orient
pearls, carbuncles, rubies, diamonds, saphires, emeraudes, blew stones,
grenates, agathes, crystal, perles, and other stones of excellency.

He has something else to do with them. I believe that you have described a
pallaice at your pleasure, and spoken by a figure. For you have a privilege you
travelers to speak at pleasure when it seems good unto you.

I speak in good sooth.

If you speak in good sooth, I answer you, that her Majesty of England has
as goodly palaces, well founded, finely set out, as sumptuous, and no less
magnificent then all those that you can name either in Spain, or in Italy:
and here are six that I will name: first the palace royal of Greenwich; then
her house called Saint James, then her mansion of Whitehall and of Richmond,
the honor of Hampt^ncourt, her house called Nonesuch, and the castle of
Windsor, all scituat within the compass of fifteen leagues, on the sweet
river of Thames, palaces (or to speak more properly) Paradices of wholesome
air, pleasure, fine soil, full of all abundance, delights, and honest
pleasures, fit for a Prince to have, not lying in one only word.

Before we go any further, tell me I pray you, what think you of this City of
London? Is it not fair and very populous?

It Is fair indeed, and likes me very well. But I cannot tell what to say yet,
for since four days, that I am here arrived, I have not stird out of my
chamber, being weary and tired with so sore traveling and running the post.

You have not been then any further in the country?

No, but if it please God this spring I will go see all the country, and the
best Cities.

You shall see then a goodly soil, very fruitful, very fertile, and of a
very temperate air, abounding in victuals, full of fair women, very
courteous and beautiful, and an Island environed with the sea.

Have they no wine here, me thinks that they drink water?

Not so sir, they drink beer, which is a drink made with barley and water
brewed together, being a drink very wholesome, and agreeing with the nature of
us Englishmen.

Ye Frenchmen, ye drink wine, you shall find wine here in great abundance.

Whence comes it?

There is that comes from France, from Spain, from Candie, and Rhenish wine
your belly full.

What sorts of wine have they?

They have Claret, red, Sack, Muscadine, and Malmesie.

Is it dear or good cheap?

The Claret, red and white are sold for six pence a quart, Sack for eight
pence, Muscadine and Malmesie for ten.

It is not to dear.

No truly, it is reasonably sold, and for an indifferent price.

Truly it is dearer in many parts of France.

London, is it a City full of merchants?

Yea sir, very full.

What merchandise is there?

Of all sorts that a man can vvish.

The merchants, where do they traffic?

Throughout all the world: In France, in Italy, in Spain, in Barbary, in
Guynea, in Bresilia, in the East and West Indies, in the East countries: in
Turkey, in Arabia, in Persia, in Tartarie, in Russia, in Poland, in Bohemia: in
Hungary, in Germany, in Frizeland, in Flanders, in Denmark, in Scotland, in
Ireland, over all Asia, Europe, Affricke, and the new America.


With what merchandise do they trade?

The best Tynne in the world is found in England: the best wool, the best
clothes and kersies; all kind of metals, as gold, silver, lead, tin,
copper, iron, steel, brass, the best saffron that is, great quantity of
hides, of grain, much vvood, and cattle, as horses, hacknies, oxen, kine,
sheep, mares.

There are very few goats.

There are no wolves, bears, lions, nor serpents, if they be not brought from
other countries.

What pastimes use they on holidaies?

All manner of pastime, as to see Comedies, and Tragedies, to leap, to danse,
fencing, beare-bayting, shooting, running, shooting in the piece, walking
abroad in the fields, rowing in boats on the water.

Is there any fair river at London?

One very fair and great.

Does it carry great vessels?

It Is very nauigable, full of all kind of fish, finely garnished on the one and
the other side with goodly buildings, fair Cities, villages and castles, and
her Majesty's Court lies almost always about it.

How call you the River?

It Is called the Thames.

Does it fall into the Sea?

Yea, it is swallowed up into the great Ocean.

Are victuals good cheap?

Sometimes they are dear enough: otherwhile very cheap: for the Thames does
bring in a great part of commodities for man's life, in ships and vessels of
burden.

There is always great plenty of bread, of flesh, cheese, butter, salt fish,
wild foul, etc.

How many Universities are there here?

There are but two: that of Cambridge, and this of Oxford.

Is there any great number of Scholars?

Sufficient.

And of learned men?

There are, and some that have great authority.

Have they fair Colleges?

Very fair and sumptuous, builded of freestone, and very costly.

Are they of great antiquity?

The histories do make mention of more then a thousand eight hundred and three
years.

Have you seen the Colleges and Universities beyond seas: are they fair?

I have been in many Universities, the most famous of Europe, and first in
those of Italy: as in Rome: in Pisa: in Bolonia: in Padua: in Turin: and then
next in those of France: as in Paris, in Orleans, at Bourges, at Rhemes in
Champaigne: at Mussi-pont: at Verdun: at Geneua, at Tournon: at Poictiers, at
Angiers: at Toulouse: and at Mont-pelier: In Germany I have seen none: very
few in Spain: but in Rome there are but four fair Colleges: that is to wit
the College of Rome, called the Seminary of Iesuists: that of Almans: the
College of Greeks: and the other called the hospital, or College of
Englishmen hard by, which is called otherwise, la casa Ingleze, standing in
strada del popolo: near the Jews market place. In Parts you have a fair
Academie, where there are many fair Colleges: as the Colleges of the
Sorbonists: of Navarre: of Montagu: of Bourgondie: of Dam-uille: of Caluy: of
Justice: of Tours: of Beau-uais: of Presles: of Lysieus: of Rhemes: of
Bon-court: of Lombards: of the Treasorers: of Narbone: of Mercy: of the
Bernard-friers: of Cluny-friers: of white Monks, of Cholets: of the Cardinal
Monk: of Plessis: of Marmontyer: of Autun: of Fortet: of the March: of S.
Barbara: of Man's: of Harcourt: of Laon: of good children: of Tournay: of
Boisie: of Bayews, Coc-keret, Minnion, of S. Dennis: of master Geruis: of S.
Michael, of the three Bishops, or of Cambray, where are read the public
Lectures by the Kings professors.

There are yet some other, but I have set down only the principal, leaving
out the Abbeys, and Monasteries.

Are these Colleges magnificent, finely built, set forth and trimmed? They are
all Colleges: but not to compare with the Colleges of Oxon. with a
Christs-Church a wonder of the world; nor to new College: nor to a S. John's:
or to an All-Soules: to the Colleges of Merton and Magdalens in Oxenford:
which are of great antiquity, very well founded all, and seem rather Palaces
for Kings, then Schools to teach the liberal Arts in, and to maintain poor
Scholars.

And he that would compare them to those of Cambridge, as to Trinity College,
or to a Saint John's, or to Kings College, Queens College, to Emmanuel and
kayes Colleges, and to diverse others where the pensioners have their revenues
to maintain them yearly, I would say, that he should be some poor blind
buzzard, that understands as little as may be, and who should deserve to be cast
into Cymmerian darkness with fetters on his heels for his so gross
ignorance.

Now sir, who governs in London?

It Is my Lord Mayor, who is her Majesty's Lieftenant, with his brethren the
Aldermen of the City.

What policy have they?

Fine policy: the customs of the country bear the greatest sway.

I have heard say, that there is a great number of malefactors, as thieves,
murderers, rouers, and Pirates.

There are many: yet they hang them every day.

They do not hang all of this race?

Some of them escape always.

Have they no other death for the malefactors?

None: but for traitors who are drawn with four horses to the place of
execution, and quartered.

It Is a great thing that you say, are there so many traitors there?

Not so: for justice does cut them off so well that they dare not devise or set
a broach any treason.

God of his holy grace preserve Queen ELIZABETH, and grant her long life and
prosperity, frustrating all the practices of her enemies, and give her happy
success of all her holy desires.

The Queens majesty does she come often to the City? Very seldom: but she
keeps with her Court always very near.

All foreign Nations truly admire the great virtues and sagenesse of her
majesty.

To confess the truth, there is no tongue able to set forth her praises: for
she is in liberality, magnificence, clemency, wisdom, mercy, beauty,
nobility, knowledge, courtesy and sagesse, the only Phoenix of the world;
endued with all the most noble virtues, wherewith the fairest and most famous
Queens in all histories of Greeks and Latins have been adorned. She may rather
be called an Angel of heaven, then an earthly creature.

Does her Majesty keep a great Court?

Truly very great and magnificent.

Does her majesty love strangers?

She loves them very well.

Does her majesty speak many languages?

She speaks eight at the least: to wit: the Greek tongue, the Latin,
Italian, French, Spanish, Scottish, Flemmish, and English: I cannot show the
other excellent virtues wherewith her majesty in all perfection is adorned:
and therefore I will produce that which the most excellent French Poet William
Salustius Lord of Bartas, has written in her praise.

These are his verses, as I have translated them into English, But what new
beam of Phoebus bright, comes dazeling ore mine eyes?
How am I at this instant made a bourgis of the Skies?
O sacred Port! what majesty so royal do I view?
What twinkling eyes like glistering stars, what face of Angels hew?
Daughters of Jove, say sisters nine, ye mistresses that taught her,
Say, is not this Elizabeth the-Great, Mineruaes daughter?
Who makes the Britain brave disdain the male-kinde Monarchy?
To crave for females Scepter, which she swaies in Britanny:
When she is moved, her neighbors round do find a fury fell,
Which seems disquieted to bring both fire and sword from hell:
Whose fear does make them sore aghast, and threats the Machine round,
With thundering tempests, rattling storms, and furious rage to drowned.
In blessed peace she does possess her kingdom where the law
Does flourish with the faith of Christ, and hold her realm in awe.
Who has not of her mother tongue the only fluent phraze:
And yet there in her eloquence the learned does amaze.
But presently she can discourse in speeches eight or nine,
In Latin, Greek, French, Spanish, Dutch, Scottish, and Florentine.
That for her birth, Imperial Rome does plead with Greece and France,
And Rhene and Arnus with all three, make mickle variance.
Bright Northren Pearl, daughter of Mars, or else well Mars his master,
The Muses and the liberal Arts, continue still to foster.
And if my verses ever chance to fly ore Ocean Maine,
Into the I Will where fair Eliza princely Nimphe does reign,
And fortunate may be to fall into those milkewhite hands,
Which sway the Scepters righteously, of many mighty lands.
Behold them with a gracious look: This favor once I crave:
Think: who your praise will pen aright, your eloquence must have.


Truly this Bartasius was a man of a rare spirit.

True it is that this Princess has governed her Kingdom in great prosperity
for the space of thirty four years, during the troubles and ruins of other
countries, having with her people been preserved from infinite dangers: and has
the tongues mentioned by the Poet very ready at command: and besides at this
present day by a singular favor of God, is known to be the Northern Perle,
recognished for a fortunate Princess in battle; her victories and happy
success being most memorable and fresh, deserve to be registered in an whole
history that the posterity shall have in reverence for ever.

God keep her majesty in his holy safeguard, bless her, and prosper her long
time, according to his good pleasure.

Sir it is time that I retire to my lodging.

I commend me unto you then.

I kiss your hands, Adue.

FINIS.
ORTHO-EPIA GALLICA. Or Le Parliament des Babillards: Id est: The Parliament of
Pratlers. M. Eliots first Book: Wherein is showed his double new Invention,
which teaches Englishmen to speak truly, speedily, and volubly the French
tongue.
Le Lever au matin. Chap. 1.
HAu garcon, dors tu vilain? debout, debout: ie te resueilleray tantost auec un
bon baston.

Ie me leave, monsieur.

Quelle heure est il?

Il est six heures.

Donnez moy mes chausses de velours verd.

Lesquelles?

C'est tout un, mes chausses rondes de satin rouge.

Baille moy vne chemise blanch premierement.

Attendez monsieur que ie la seiche au feu.

Espousette bien mon chapeau and mon manteau.

Nettoye mes souliers.

Tens moy mon pourpoint de Satin noir, car ie le veux porter auec mes chausses,
de velours cramoisin a la Venicienne.

Voyla voz chausses vostre pourpoint and vostre chemise, monsieur.

Tu m'as apporte vne chemise a femme, vilain, tu ne scais pas ce que tu fais.

Pardonnez moy, monsieur, s'il vous plaist, ie me suis abuse, c'est la chemise
de mademoiselle.

Garcon maladuise, tu me feras resentir la chemise a femme tout ce iourd huy
and demain.

Voyla la vostre monsieur.

Ou sont mes bas de chausses?

Mes souliers ou sont ils?

Les voyla mons.

Voulez vous porter vos souliers a simple semelle auiourd'huy?

Non, non, ie veux porter mes souliers a double and triple semelle.

Quel temps fait il dehors?

Il y fait fort beau temps.

Donnez moy donc mes mulles and mes escarpins, mais ou sont mes chaussons?

Ie les vay cercher.

Va me querir le chausse-pied.

I'ay bless mon pied, il me faut aller mes souliers en pantofle.

Ou as tu mis ma ceinture and mes iartiers?

Les voyla en la fenestre derriere le mirouer.

Aid moy attacher mes esguillettes.

Apporte vne aiguiere de l'eau net pour me la uer les mains, la face and mes
yeux.

La touaille pour essuyer mes mains?

Tenez monsieur, en voyla vne.

Asteure que ie suis habille, me voyla pressed a desieusner.

Le Lever o mateen. Chap. 1.
HO garssoon, dortu veelein? deboo, deboo: ziete reue-lhere tan-tot-tauec-keun
boon batoon.

zIe me leveh moonseewr.

Ke-leur et-til?

Il-e see-zewres.

Done moe' mes shosseh de veloor vert.

Le-keles?

Set-toot-tewn, mes shosseh roondeh de sateen roo-zieh.

Ba-lheh moe' vneh shemee-zeh blaun-sheh pre-mheremant.

Attande moonseewr ke zie la se-sho few.

Epoozetteh bee-en moon shapeaw e moom manteaw.

Nettoyeh mes soolierz.

Tan moe' moom poorpoin de Sateen noe'r, car zie le view porter-auec mes shosseh
de veloor cramoe'zeen-a la Veneesee-anneh.

Voe'la vos shosses, voter poorpointe voter shemeezeh, moonseewr.

Tu ma-zapporte vneh shemeeza fammeh, veelein, tu ne s'e pas se ke tu fez.

Pardonne moe' moonseewr, sil voo plet, zie me sweezabuze, se la shemeezeh de
mad'moe'zeleh.

Garssoon mal-a-vee-ze tu me fera resanteer la shemeeza fammeh too-se zioordwee

Oo soon me ba de shosses?

Mes soulier-zoo soont-ilz?

Le voe'la mons.

zIe bless moom pee-e, il me fo-tal-ler mes soolier-zan pantofle.

Oo a-tu me ma seenture me-ziartierz?

Le voe'la an la feneter derrhe-reh le mee-roo-er.

Edeh moe' attasher me-ze-gee-lhe-tes.

Apportu-ne-gee-ereh de leaw netteh poor me laver le meinz, la fase me-zeewz.

La too-a-lheh poor-esswee-er me meinz?

Tene moonseewr, an voe'la ew-neh.

Asteur ke zie swee-za-bee-lhe, me vla pret-ta de-ziewner.

Pour saluer les hommes. Chap. 2.
BOn iour a tous mes bons seigneurs.

Bon iour and bon an seigneur Claude.

Bon soir and bonne sante a trestous.

Dieu vous doint bon iour compere.

Bon iour boniour compagnon d'eschole.

Ie vous en rends pour quatre quatorze and pour cinq cinquante,

Bon iour a vostre seigneurie tresexcellente.

Comment se port monsieur le Prince de Conde auec madame la Princess?

Comment se sent Monseigneur le Duc de Nemours and madame la Duchess?

Monsieur le Marquis de Saluce auec Madame la Marquise se portent-ils bien?

Monsieur le Comte de Soissons, and Madame la Comtesse sont-ils en bon estat?

Monsieur le Marshall d'Anuille and son friar le Seneschal de Lyonnois se
recommendent bien humblement a la bonne grace de vostre tres-illustre
seigneurie.

Et comment vous portez vous seigneur Nicho^as? Comment vous va? Comment vous
est-il?

Ie me port tresbien la Dieu mercy, and vous?

Ie me sens malade de la bourse, bien dispos de corpse and de la bouche.

Comment se port la sante?

Ie suis tout galiard and dispos asteure, sauf que i'ay eu un acces de fiebure
qui m'a fort debilite.

Courage* courage, vous vous porterez bien par la grace de Dieu.

Ie suis pressed a vous fair plaisir.

Et vous, vous sentez vous bien aussi?

Ie me sens enter l'enclume and le marteau. Comme vous voyez.

Sain comme un poisson pourri.

Il me plaist certes de vous veoir en bonne disposition.

Ie vous en mercy bien humblement.

Ie vous en rends graces tresaffectueusement.

Ie vous en remercie autant qu'il m'est possible.

Tous noz bons amiss se portent ils bien?

Ils se portent tresbien trestous auec tous leur petits enfans and parents.

Si ie peu fair quelque chose pour vous commandez moy franchement.

Ie vous baise les mains mons. Ie suis le vostre, ie suis a vostre gentil
commandment and service.

Et moy au vostre certes. Venez a la Court, ie vous y feray le plus grand
honneur qu'il me sera possible.

Ie seray arrouse auec l'eau beniste de la Court, c'est a dire, i'auray un
deluge de ceremonies, mais autant des queues de singes que de disners and
desieuners.

Les tonneaux vuides font tousiours plus de bruit que les pleins.

A folles parolles oreilles sourdes.

Le pourmeiner. Chap. 3.
ALLons nous pourmeiner.

Ie veux desieusner deuant que sortir de la maison.

Allons, allons, nous desieunerons quand nous sommes de retour.

Rien, rien, ie veux manger un morceau du pain, and boire un doigt du vin, puis
apres allons y la ou voudriez.

Vous deburiez desieuner deuant que soyez leave. Ce seroit vne coustume nouuelle.

Pardonnez moy, plusieurs dames de Londres font de mesme, and quand else ont
bien desieusne se recouchent pour s'en rendormir la dessus.

Pensez vous que i'eusse remarque leur mode en cela?

Ie n'en scay rien.

He! le Diable vous emporte, vous estes un gaudisseur, vous estes un bavard.

Vous estes un porc d'espi pour tout potage.

Pour vous confesser la verite: c'est a craindre que tells femmes planteront
les corns au front de leur maris, s'ils ne se donnent bien guard.

Ou auez vous veu un coronard seul enter mill hommes?

Non pas un seul, mais bien un cent.

Ou cela?

En ce microcosme des corns and coronards.

Les boeufs portent corns, and les veaux cornettes.

Vous estes clerc, ie voy bien: mais a propos ou irons nous pourmeiner?

A la montagne de la Tour.

Aux champs saint George de la l'eau.

A la Dogane pour entrer la charge d'vne nauire.

A un village pour manger de la cresme.

Au Cemitiere saint Pol pour achepter un liver nouueau.

A la Cour, a la halle blanch.

Au ieu de paume pres les Carmes.

A la chasse d'vne biche.

A la cour de Finances.

Au Palais de Ooest-minster, pour parler a un aduocat, pour prendre aduis d'vn
conseillier.

A l^ test Moresque pour liurer vnes lettres.

A la prison de Neuue-porte pour donner l'ausmone aux pauures prisonniers pour
l'honneur de Dieu A la Bourse pour entendre les nouuelles de France.

Ou vous trou-veray-ie enuiron les douze heures?

Ie seray la bas au Change, ou me pourmeinant parmi les Italiens, ou troquant
auec les Francois, ou deuisant enter les Anglois, ou beuuant d'autant auec les
Flamands au chapeau Cardinalesque: and si ie ne suis pas en ces endroitz, vous
me trouuerez la haut en la salle quadrangulaire folastrant auec les belles
lingieres.

Adieu iusques a tantost donc.

Iusques a reuoir, Adieu.

Le poormener. Chap. 3.
ALLoon no poormener.

zIe view de-ziewner deuan ke sorteer de la mezoon.

Alloon, alloon, no de-ziewneroon kan noos soommeh de retoor.

Ree-en, ree-en, zie view man-zier-ewn morseaw du pein, e boer-ewn doe' du veen,
pui-za-prez-alloonzee la oo voodriez.

Voo devrie de-ziewner deuan ke soye leave. Se seroe't-tu-neh cootumeh nooveleh.

Pardonne moe', pluzeewr dameh de Loondre foon de memeh, e kant-elezoon bee-en
de-ziewne se recoosheh pour s'an randormeer la dessewz.

Panse voo ke ziewsseh remarke lewr modan sela?

zIe nan se ree-en.

He! le Dee-able voo-zamporteh, voo-zette-zewn godissewr, voo-zette-zewn

La Bourse.
Englishman. God morrow to you all my masters. Castilian. Buenos dias ayan
vuestras mercedes* Portugois. Sennores hablemos todos buen Cristiano. Italian.
Buon Giorno e buon anno Signori. French. Ie n'entends pas Latin, monsieur.
NE scauez vous pas parler Francois?

I'en scay quelque peu monsieur.

Quelles nouuelles par la France?

Pas autre chose que ie scache: tousiours la guerre.

Qui bruit par le monde?

Ie n'entends rien que ce soit.

Scauez vous rien de nouueau?

Que dit-on de Barbary, d'Italie, d'Espagne, de Turquie?

N'y a il rien de bonnes nouuelles?

Lon parle de la grand' Carraque que noz Anglois prindrent l'autre iour des
Espagnols retournants des Indes Orientalles.

La flotte est elle revenue de Bourdeaux?

Vous n'entendez rien des nauires de Tripoli and Axanthe?

Ou demeure la post ordinaire a Calais, a Bruges, a Anuers?

Il demeure en la rue des Iuifs. A quelle enseigne?

Au Lion blanc, au singe marmottant.

Est ce pas vis a vis le Lycorne?

C'est un petit plus outre: deca la cheure and le cheual rouge, tout contre le
Taureau noir pres la test du chien au pot.

Comment pourray-ie fair pour luy liurer un packet des lettres?

Vous le trouuerez sur la Bourse enuiron les onze heures and demye.

Mais quand s'en ira il, le scaues vous?

Il s'embarquera paraduenture auec la premiere maree.

Ou est le vent? Il est en midy. Il luy est donc contraire: il estoit a ee
matin enter l'oueste and le Nord.

Il me faut quarente lingots d'estaing de Cornouaille pour enuoier en Barbary,
and deux cents draps larges pour enuoyer a un merchant Focquer d'Alemagne.

Voyci un merchant Anglois qui vous en fournira bien.

Messer Paolo, quelz commoditez apporte le Gallion de Venice?

Sa charge est des huiles, des vins, des soyes and draps de soye.

N'y a il point des Orenges, Limons, Cytrons, Figues and raysins?

Tout cela vient d'Espagne non pas de l'Italie.

Me voulez vous vendre dix tonneaux du Muscadel a term?

I'aymeroye mieulx les auoir donnez a Palerme.

Quand seray-ie pay pour les huiles que vous eustes de moy dernierement.

Apres pasques: si pouuez auoir la patience.

Cancaro madonna stiamo fresch^.

Argent, argent, ou gage, ou ie vous feray mettre en cage. Ie n'entends pas
vostre langage.

Voyla vne belle lettre d'assignation.

Ie demand pleige, caution, argent content, non pas obligation.

Voyla donc vostre argent: contez le, voyla des beaux Angelots. Cecy me semble
un demon, cestuyla est rongne.

Pour combien me donnez vous cest Angelot rongne?

Pour deux diablottins.

Au Diable soit le Diable. Ie n'en veu point.

Voyla vne autre piece d'or. Elle ne pese pas. Ce Souueraing est soude. Ces
realles d'Espaigne ne sont pas de bon alloy.

Pour combien me payez vous ceste Portugaise?

Pour deux Espagnolles. Pour combien cest escu au soleil?

Pour deux targues.

Il est trop legier de six grains.

Pour co^bien ce noble a la Rose?

Pour deux vilains.

Il est de bas or. Pour combien ce double-doublon?

Pour deux simples baronnages Pour co^bien ce Philippusd'or?

Pour deux Henricus a la barbe longue.

Il semble ex tribu Leui. Il est trop court. Il n'est pas de mise. Il est faux.

Ie ne l'ay pas forge. Ie vouldrois que les oreilles du fauxmonnoyeur qui les a
falsifiez fussent cloves a ce pilier.

Changez moy ces pieces legieres. Ie n'en veux point de ces monnoyes.

Vous estes bien curieux a receuoir argent. Voyla d'autre argent. Que i'aye vne
quittance de vostre main.

La voyla.

Ie suis pay asteure. Adieu.

MOntons au dessus pour marchander quelque chose de ces belles filles.

Ho un Paon! Ou est Quintus Hortensius qui l'aimoit tant?

Tens icy le plat aux Olives: I'ay veu un quidam qui deuoroit les noyaux

De quelle ceruoise donc? De la plus simple que soit: car les autres re^dent

Ie vous y feray raison icy.

Quel vin vous plaist il boyre monsieur?

Il n'y a chose plus griefue que mourir de soif en un banquet.

Du vin ho! Dernierement que ^'estoye a Rome, ie beu chez le Cardinal Caraffa

Le muscadel me plaist grandement.

Aussi fait-il aux dames Angloises.

Ce vin commence a se decliner, a s'enaigrir and a moisir.

Les vins d'Espagne and d'Italie portent bien l'eaue, and si sont de guard.

Verses moy de l'eau iusques a mi-verre.

Vous baptisez vostre vin and le faites bon Chrestien.

Cela n'est pas mal fait.

Vous gastez le vin y mettant trop de l'eaue: Ie ne boyray pas auec vous. Ie
l'aime quand il est simple pur, and net, non pas brouille comme on fait en
plusieurs endroits du monde pour fair d'vn tonneau deux.

C'est peu de cas cela.

Pis font les Tauerniers de Londres qui mettent de la chaux, du soulphre, de
miel, de l'alun, and autres chooses plus laides a dire, and n'y a rien plus
dommageable aux corpse humains: lesquels on deuvroit chastier publiquement comme

principallement les gouttes.

Messieurs vous ne mangez rien. Ie vous pry de boire and me fair rayson
d'autant.

Beuuons amiss: il fait certes hui beau boire: aussi fait il tous les iours.

Beuuons, ie boy de bien bon coeur a vous and soyez les tresbien venus.

N'ayez pas peur que vin and viures icy faillent: car quand le ciel seroit

Beuuons donc ensemble par bon accord en charity.

Ie suis a vous. Vous estes a moy. Ie boy a vous, vous me ferez raison, s'il

Hem, ehem!

Il est bon and frais assez, comme vous diriez sur le commencement du second

A chacun n'est octroye de boire de si bon:

A chacun n'est octroye d'habiter Corinthe.

(...), c'est a dire, En vin verite.

Escoutez mon amy, ie te diray vne chose a l'oreille: Ne le die a person, si
tu m'aimes: Il demeurera secret enter nous deux: c'est* que ie trouue le vin
meilleur and plus a mon goust sauoureux que ne soulois: plus que ne soulois ie
crains la rencontre du mauuais vin: and pour vous en dire la pure verite
l'odeur du vin combien plus est friant, riant, priant, tant plus celeste and
delicieux est il que l'huile.

C'est parle en clerc cela. Ie vous en conteray bien d'autres. Attendez un peu
que ie hume quelque traict de ceste bouteille: voyci mon vray and seul Helicon.
Voyci ma fontaine Caballine. C'est icy mon unique Enthusiasme.

Icy beuuant ie delibere, ie discourse, ie resouls and concluds. Apres
l'Epilogue ie ry, i'escry, ie compose, ie boy.

Ennius le pere des Poets latins beuuant escriuoit, escriuant beuuoit.

Aeschylus (si a Plutarque foy auez in Symposiacis) beuuoit composant,
composoit beuuant.

Homere iamais n'escriuist a ieusne.

Caton iamais n'escriuist qu' apres boire. Afin que ne me dites ainsi viure
sans exemple des bien louez and mieux prisez.

Ya il quelq'un qui veut disputer auec moy de ces problemes insolubles de la

Ces sont haultes matieres and sciences profondes.

Saint Dame il faut rire: Tire, baille page, vin, boutte, de par le Diable
boutte, ie veux boire pour ma part plus de vingt cinq ou trente trois muis
deuant que mourir.

N'y a il plus vin? Nous sommes donc au sec: and aux deserts de l'Arabie.

Il fait chaud. O que suis altere. Sicut terra sine aquae.

Ie croy que person de vous autres Beuueurs n'en doubt.

Y a il force vins en Gascogne stannee?

I'espere donc que nous autres Anglois trouuerons remedes infallibles contre
toutes alterations and soifs.

Ie boy a tous. Vous me semblez vrays Chrestiens: car ie ne boy point a ces
chiens des Turcs, Mahometains, ie les renie and renonce pour des villains.

La raison pourquoy?

Ie vous conteray comment ces diables des Turcs sont bien malheureux de ne
boire goutte du vin. Si autre mal n'estoit en l'Alcoran de Mahomet, encores ne
me mettrois-ie point de sa loy.

Digne d'eternelle memoire and louange sempiternelle fut le saint homme Noah
(auquel sommes obligez and tenuz dece qu'il nous planta la vigne, don't nous
vient ceste nectarique, delicieuse, celeste, ioyeuse, deifique liqueur. (Le

puissance d'iceluy.) Auez vous bien le tout e^ tendu?

Beuuez donc un bon coup sans eau: car si ne le croyez, non fay-ie sit-elle.

Ou sont ces diables des Gregeois qui au temps d'Alexandre furent beuueurs
tres-illustres? O les pauures hairs sont morts.

Nous lisons que ces gentiles demons Italiens ont bien beu autre fois: and
principalement au temps de Iules Caesar quand ilz firent leurs cheuaux, muletz,
and iuments boire d'autant.

Ie suis, croyez moy, bon gautier, and bon compagnon.

I'ayme boire net, and ie mange volontiers sale, and si i'ayme boire du
meilleur, si fait tout homme de bien dea.

Iamais homme noble ne hait le bon vin.

Beuuons, beuuo^s donc, comme font les chameaux and dromadaires en la Carauane,
beuuants pour la soif passee, pour la soif present, and pour la soif future.

Ainsi feist Hercules.

Treues de soif, treues de faim.

Ie ne suis plus fasche Dieu mercy and vous. Ie suis gay comme un Papegay,
ioyeux comme un esmerillon, alaigre comme un Papillon.

Veritablement il est escrit par vostre beau Euripides, and Silenus beuueur
memorable le dit: Furieux est and de bon sens ne iouist:
Qu conque boit and ne s'en resiouist.


Veu qu'il y a long temps que i'ay verse a l'eschole d'Apollon, and du font
Cabalin beu a plein godet enter les ioyeuses Muses: depuis ce temps la ie
trouue ce nectar divine, ce vin precieux, ce muscadet delicat.

Compere tout beau, vous faites rage de humer.

Ie done au diable: tu n'as pas trouue tes petits beuuereaux de Londres qui ne
beuuent en plus qu' un poincon.

Vous auez bon gosier pour bien aualler.

O compagnon si ie montasse aussi bien que i'aualle, ie fusse desia au dessus
la sphere de la Lune auec Empedocles.

Mais ie ne scay que Diable cecy veut dire: Ce vin est si fort bon and
delicieux. Plus i'en boy, plus i'ay soif.

Ie croy que l'ombre de ces hanaps engendre les alterez comme la Lune fait les
catarreux, Ceste test de leuraut, est bonne pour les goutteux.

Nous ne mangerons guere d'oisons ceste annee.

I'auois bien desieusne: mais pource ne mangeray-ie point moins. Car i'ay un
estomac paue and creux comme la botte saint Benoist.

Ha mon amy, baille de ce cochon.

Diauol, il n'y a plus de moust, ie renie ma vie, ie meurs de soif.

Par my fie commere ie ne puis entrer en mes bettes.

Vous estes morfondue, mamie. Voire. Parlons de boire. Ie ne boy qu' a mes
heures, comme la mule du Pape I'ay grand foif.

Qui fut le premier, soif ou beuuerie? Beuuerie. Car Priuatio praesupponit
habitum.

Ie suis clerc, vous di-ie.

Foecundi calices quem non fecere disertum?
Chantons, beuuons, entonnons, ou est mon entonnoir?

Quoy? ie ne boy que par procuration.

Mouillez vous pour seicher, ou seichez vous pour mouiller?

Par ma foy, ie n'entends pas la Rhetorique. De la prattique ie m'en aid
quelque peu.

Baste, ie mouille, ie humecte, ie boy and tout de peur de mourir.

Beuuez tous-iours vous ne mourrez iamais.

Si ie ne boy, me voyla mort.

Pour conclusion de mon harangue ie vous diray, que quant a moy ie cuide que
soye descendu de quelque rich Roy ou Prince au temps iadis. Car onques ne
veistes homme qui eust plus graude affection d'estre Roy and rich que moy,
afin de fair grande chere, pas ne trauailler, point me soucier and bien
enrichir mes amiss and tous gens de bien and scauoir.

C'est assez beu pour vne fois. Mangeons, mangeons, estendez les bras, ne
craigniez pas le boucon de Lombard Nous auons assez mange pour vne fois certes.

Vous auez fait un banquet fort sumptueux.

On n'a pas touch ces chappons rostis.

Ne mangez vous pas d'un faisandeau* messires?

Pigeonneaux?

Ah! des poissons and de la chair en vne mesme table! La mer se meslera par mi
la terre, les medecins le defendent.

Mais les medecins le veuillent.

I'ay failli, ie deuoye dire que la medecin le defend, and non pas les
medecins.

Quant a moy ie parleray des poissons, ie n'en mangeray pas.

Deseruez la table, ho: and apportez le fruit, Il y a vne merueilleuse variete

Voyla un pesche coing.

Voyci certes des belles pommes and des poires delicates.

Faites bonne chere messieurs, and soyez allaigres and ioyeux.

Seruez vous mesmes, car ie ne sers person.

Aions de fromage.

Il faut que ie mange un peu de fromage.

En voyla de fort bon. cestuy-ci est de iument.

C'est du laict d'Asnesse Phrygienne ou Sicilienne.

Ce fromage combien qu'il soit d'Angleterre est plein des troux and vermineux.

Il serabon aux Flamands donc.

Ce Parmesan est bien amasse and plus frais que cestuy-la d'Ollande, qui est
creux aussi.

Il n'est pas de Parme, ains de Plaisance.

O que ce fromage done bien l'appetit a boyre.

Buvons donc pour gaigner les pardons.

Messieurs si i'eusse este seur de vostre venue. Vous auez fait trop de despens.
