Mouillez moy doucement.

Vous couperay ie les cheueux?

Voulez vous fair raser la barbe?

Vous laueray ie le col, la poictrine and l'estomac?

Vous nettoyeray-ie les dents?

Garcon, ou sont mes-cizeaux?

Baillez moy ce peigne d'Iuoire.

Aiguisez un peu le rasoir.

Vous coupperay-ie les moustaches?

Vne cure-dent and cure-oreille ho.

Vous estes quasi accoustre.

Prenez le miroir, and mirez vous.

Me voyla bien.

Sambredeigne, ie port vne mine bien fiere and felonne.

Tien pour ton vin. Adieu.

The vprising in the morning. Chap. 1.
WHat boy, slepest thou villain? up up: I shall wake thee by and by with a
good cudgel.

I rise sir.

What a clock is it?

It is six a clock.

Give me my green velvet breeches.

Which?

It is all one, my round red satin ones.

Give me a clean shirt first.

Stay sir, till I air it at the fire.

Brush well my hat and my cloak.

Make clean my shoes.

Reach me my black Satin doublet, for I will wear it with my erimsin
velvet venitians.

There be your breeches, your doublet, and your shirt, sir.

Thou have brought me a smock villain, thou know not what thou do.

Pardon me sir if it please you, I am deceived, it is my mistress smock.

VVretchlesse boy thou will make me smell of the smock all to day and to
morrow.

There is your shirt sir.

Where be my netherstocks?

Where be my shoes?

There they are sir.

Will you wear your single-soled shoes to day?

No, no, I will wear my shoes of double and three soles.

What wether is it abroad?

It is very fair weather.

Give me then my pumpes and my pantofles: but where are my sockes?

I go to seek them.

Go fetch me the shooing horn.

I have hurt my foot, I must go slip-shood.

Where have thou laid my girdle and my garters?

There they are in the window behind the looking glass.

Help me to tie my points.

Bring a basin of fair water to vvash my hands, face and mine eyes.

The towell to dry my hands.

Here sir, there is one.

Now that I am dressed, I am ready to go to breake-fast.

To salute men. Chap. 2.
GOod morrow to you all my masters.

Good day and good year to you signior Claudius.

God night and a thousand to every body.

God give you good morrow Gossip.

Goodmorrow goodmorrow Scholefel^ow.

I give you again fourteen goodmorrowes for four, and for five fifty.

God-morrow to your excellency.

How does my Lord the Prince of Condy, with madame the Princess?

How does my Lord the Duke of Nemours, with my Lady the Duchess feel
themselves?

Mounsieur the Marquis of Saleuce with Madame the Marquise are they in good
health?

My Lord the Count Soissons, with my Lady the Countess, are they well?

Monsieur Martial D'anuille and his brother Lord Steward of the Province of
Lions, have them heartily commended to your most renowned excellency.

And how do you seignior Nicholas,? How goes the world with you? How is it
with you?

I do very well, God be thanked, and you?

I feel my self sick in my purse, lusty of body, and ready to go to
breakefast.

How does your body? I am all gallant and lusty now, saving that I have had a
fit of an Ague, which has pulled me down.

Courage, courage, man, you shall do well by Gods grace.

I am ready to do you a pleasure.

And you, are you lusty man?

I am in a pecke of troubles. As you see.

As whole as a rotten fish.

I am glad truly to see you in good health.

I thank you most heartily.

I thank you with all my soul.

I thank you a thousand times.

All our good friends are they well?

They are all very well, with all their little barnes and kindred.

If I may do any thing for you command me freely.

I thank you sir. I am yours, I am at your gentle commandment and service.

And I at yours truly. Come to the Court, I will do you the greatest credit
that I may possibly.

I shall be sprinkled with the Court holy water, that is to say, I shall have
a deluge of ceremonies, but as many apes tails as dinners and breakefasts.

Empty tons make more noise then full vessels.

To foolish words stopped ears.

The walking. Chap. 3.
LEt us go walk.

I will break my fast before I go a step out of doors.

Come, come, we will go to break fast when we come again.

Not I, I will eat a morsel of bread and drink a little wine, then have
with you whether you will.

You should break fast before you be up. That were a new guise.

Not so, many London mistresses do so, and when they have well broken their
fast, lay themselves down again to take a nap upon it.

Think you that I had marked their manner therein?

I can not tell.

Go go, the Dill take you, you are a scoffer, you are a frumper, you are a
hedgehogge for all sauce.

To tell you the troth, it is to be feared that such women will plant horns
on their husbands foreheads if they look not unto them well.

Where have you seen one cuckold alone among a thousand men?

Never one alone but one hundred.

Where I pey you?

In this microcosme of horns and cuckolds.

Oxen have horns and calves cornets.

You are a clarke, I perceive well, but to the purpose, where shall we go
fetch our walk?

To Tower Hill.

To saint Georges fields beyond the water. To the Custom House, to enter the
lading of a ship.

To a village to eat some cream.

To Paul's churchyard to buy a new book.

To the Court, to White Hall.

To the Tennis-court in Blacke-friers.

To the hunting of a Stagge.

To the Exchecker office.

To Westminster hall to talk with an Attornie, to take counsel of a
Counselor.

To the Sarasens head to deliver a letter.

To the prison of Newgate, to give alms to the poor prisoners for Gods sake.

To the Exchange, to hear the news out of France.

Where shall I find you about twelve a clock?

I will be below in the Change, either vvalking among the Italians, or troking
with the French, or prattling amongst our English, or carroussing with the
Flemings at the Cardinals hat: And if I be not in these places, you shall find
me above in the Pawn, devising with the fair Semsters.

Farewell then, till by and by.

Till we meet, Adieu.

The Exchange. Chap. 4.
CAn you not speak French?

I can a little, sir.

What news in France?

None that I can tell, still war, war.

What bruit in the world?

I hear nothing at all.

Know you no news?

What say they from Barbary, Italy, from Spain, and Turkey?

Is there no good news?

They talk of the great Carricke which our English took the other day of the
Spaniards, coming from the East Indies.

Can you tell me when the ships shall set sail toward Moscouie?

The Ambassador is he departed toward Constantinople?

Is the Fleet returned from Bourdis?

You hear no news of the Tripoly and Xanthe ships?

Where dwells the ordinary post to Calis, to Bruges, to Antuerpe?

He dwells in the Iewrie.

At what sign?

At the white Lion, at the grenning Iackanapes.

Is it not right over against the Unicorn?

It is a little further, on this side the Goat and the red horse, just over
against the black Bull, near the Dogs head in the pot.

How shall I do to deliver him a packet of letters?

You shall find him upon the Exchange about half an hour after eleven a
clock.

But when will he depart, can you tell?

He will embarke peradventure with the first tide?

Where is the vvind? It is in the South. It is against him then, it was this
morning North North-east.

I want forty blocks of tin of Cornwall, to send into Barbary, and two
hundred broad Clothes to send to a Focquer merchant of Germany.

Here is an English merchant, who will furnish you very well.

Signior Paulo what commodities brings this Gallion from Venice?

His lading is of oils, of vvines, of silks and veluets.

Is there no Oranges, Lemmons, Cytrons, Figs, and raisins?

All that comes from Spain, not from Italy.

Will you sell me ten tons of Muscadine for term?

I had rather have given them away at Palermo.

When shall I be paid for the oils that you had of me last?

After Easter, if you can have the patience.

Cancaro madonna stiamo fresci.

Argent, argent, or gage, or I will make you go to cage. I understand not your
language.

There is a fair letter of Attornie.

I demand pledge, bail, ready money, no obligation.

There is then your money, count it, there be fair Angels. This seems to me a
Demon, that is clipped.

For how much give you me this clipped Angel?

For two little devils.

The Devil take the Devil. I will none of them.

There is another piece of Gold. It is not weight. This Souueraigne is
saudred. These Spanish reals are not good metal.

For how much pay you me this Portegue?

For two spanish gentlewomen.

For how much this French Crown?

For two sheilds.

It is to light by six grains.

For how much this Rose noble?

For two villains.

It is of base Gold. For how much this double Ducate?

For two simple baronries.

For how much this bald pate?

For two Henry long beards.

It seems to lack weight. It is to light. It is not to be put away. It is
false.

I have not falsified it. I would that the ears of the coyner who has
falsified them were nailed to this pillar.

Change me these light pieces. I will none of these small moneys.

You are very curious in receiving of money. There is other silver. Let me have
a quittance of your hand.

There it is.

I am paid now. Farewell.

The Pawn. Chap. 5.
LEt us go above to buy some thing of these fair maidens.

What seek you sir?

Come here my friend: see here fine ruffes, falling bands, handkerchers,
sockes, coiffes, and cuffes, wrought with gold and silver. Have I nothing
which likes you? I will use you well. Would you have any fine Holland? Any
Cambricke, I have very fine, and of all prices.

Hark my love will you take a pint of wine?

Thanks sir, not now.

Fine Venice Glasses, French garters, Spanish gloves, sweet, Flanders knives,
fine Silk stocks of Italy.

What want you Gentlewoman? what lack ye?

Show me a Peach colored Netherstocke.

There is a very fine hose, the price is an Angel, at at word.

Will you take a noble?

I cannot truly. There is a pair of incarnate take them for eight shillings.

You are too dear.

Will you see a good hat sir?

Lack you a good hat or a cap?

I seek for a Beuer.

There is one which will fit you just, with the feather.

I wear no plume.

It becomes you very well.

It is too large and too great for me.

It is after the Babilonian fashion, and the feather after the Polonian flant.

It is all the fashion now a days.

Show me another after the French fashion, with a flat crown.

Will you see one of Spanish wool? with a Cypres band, pinkt with taffetas,
and finely trimde on the new cut.

Let me see it.

Let us come to a price.

The price is reasonable: give me a baldpate.

Hold your hand, you shall have neither more nor less.

Take you any double Ducates?

Come, I will pay you in Portegues, or in lack an Apes pence.

Hem! ho: hear ye?

Come hither, buy something of me, Northren man.

Ah my love, I have not money enough to bestow.

You sir, what want ye?

Is there no high shoes here abouts?

Come here my friend: here is a pair which will fit you just.

Will you buy a pretty wench to carry into the North with you?

By my troth I no.

What lackest thou welsh boy?

Thou liedst, I am a Pilchard.

Wallon, you honest man of Wales, buy something of me.

Soft and fair gallant.

Soft and fair French Pig.

You anger my Lords of the Parliament.

What want ye sir?

What would you buy mistress?

What lackst thou fellow?

What will it please you to buy Gentleman?

What seek you honest man?

Come hither: come to me.

I will sell you a peniworth.

I want nothing.

I will buy nothing.

I seek nothing.

I will have nothing of you. I stay here for one.

I walk here for my pleasure.

The drunken men's Banquet. Chap. 6.
HO that I am glad! we must laugh and be merry. Let us make good cheer.

Let us cast away care.

Come make a good fire.

Wash the glasses.

Hunt out the dogs.

Cut bread for the potage.

Set abroach a new pipe of Wine.

Set packing the poor.

Give them that they would have.

Set some chaires.

Lay the cloth.

Cover the Table.

Turn the spit.

Bring clean napkins.

Baste the roast meat.

Is the dinner ready?

See our guestes where they come.

Sir you come looked for. In good time.

Yea: but I played the villain: for I was bidden to dinner, and not to make you
stay for me.

Be ye all the very velcome masters.

Come near, if it please you mistress, sister, Aunte, Granmother, my Niece.

Come in, come in. Here Is much ado with these wives: welcome truly.

Cousen come in for Gods sake.

Why have ye not brought my little couzin with you?

Sister show them the way, the other will follow you.

Do not push me for I am with child.

Tib, Jane, Sib, reach your uncle here a basin of water, and a towell. Lets
wash all together.

Come to Table masters. Sit down my magnificent Damoisels.

O little boy say us grace.

God give us of his grace:

And in his Paradise a place.

Draw your knives.

Help that Gentlewoman. Set there the Venizon pastie.

What are these pasties of?

This is of Stags flesh.

This is of a Do: and that as I think is venison of a vvilde boar.

There, there, begin where it shall please you.

Ye see your cheer. Welcome all.

Bring the roast. This piece of beef is raw. Take it away.

Why bring ye nothing else out of the kitchen? Ye make us stay well here.

Help your selves in the mean time.

Cut up this Hare.

Eat of this Turkey Cock. Open the lid of that Pigeon pie.

Cut of that shoulder of mutton.

Dismember that Capon. Be doing with these Rabbets.

Set in order on the table the Hens, Partridges, Conies, Cranes, Feasants,
Larkes, and Woodcockes.

What sause shall we have for these dishes?

There is Orenges, Citrons, and Olives kept in pickle.

What shall we do with these great birds, the Goose, the Swan, and the
Peacock? There is no more place to set them in.

Show them only and carry them back to the kitchen.

What a Peacock? Where is Quintus Hortensius who did love it so well?

Cut me a bit or two of this gammon, to the end that I^ drink more
courageously this first glass. O the brave gammon of ba^^n! where is it? It Is
invisible. I see it not. It Is a Moguntine gammon.

Reach hither the dish with Olives: I saw a man who did swalow down these
Oliue-stones, as an Estridge does iron.

Now I drink to you all, and be ye the very well welcome.

Where are our Crystal glasses? Will you drink after the Greeks guise in
this great goblet? I will drink after the French fashion in this glass, And
I after the English manner out of this silver cup.

Bring me a little beer in this Samian viole: The which I take to be very
good this hot vveather, to refresh the body.

Of what beer then? Of the smallest that is: for the strong make the wit
dull, heavy and gross, and do feed the body too fat.

Power this cup full. To you cousin Geneuefa.

This damsel is shamefast. She brides it.

No displeasure to you sir,

I drink to you.

I will pledge you here.

What wine pleases it you to drink sir?

Will you drink Gascon wine?

Whence comes this so bloody and black wine? It Is Orleans wine. I would
rather drink of this small wine of Rochell.

There Is very good claret: which turns the vvind mill near it.

What wine drink ye cousin? A sack of Spain: which wets well, and washes
the brain.

I had rather drink a cup of Rhenish wine: for it make a man speak Latin fine.

There Is nothing more grievous then to die for thirst in a banquet.

Some wine here h^! when I was at Rome, I drank of most excellent vvines in
the Cardinal Caraffaes Celler. I drank Romanesco, Greco, lachryma Christi:
sweet wine, sharp, mild, and green, for I was very familier with his
yeoman of Celler.

Muscadine likes me very well.

So does it our English dames also.

This wine begins to wane, to be sour, and wax mustie.

The wine of Spain and Italy bear well their water, and will keep well
beside.

Power me my glass half full of water.

You christen your wine, and make it a good Christian.

That's not done amiss.

You mar the wine, putting too much water in: I will not drink with you. I
love it when it is simple, pure, and neat, not brewed, as they do in many
parts of the world, to make of one tun two.

That is a small matter.

Worse do the vintners of London, who put in lime, brimstone, honey, allume,
and other more beastly things to be spoken, and nothing is more hurtful to
men's bodies, whom men ought to chastise publicly as thieves and murderers:
for thence proceed infinite maladies, and specially the goutes.

Masters ye eat nothing, I pray you drink and pledge me a carouse.

Lets drink ho: truly it is to day a fair weather to drink in, so is it every
day.

Drink we, I drink to you with all my heart, and be ye the very well welcome.

Fear not least wine and victuals fail here: for when the heaven should be
of brass, and the earth iron, yet wine should not want us, were it for seven,
yea for eight years, A longer time then the famine lasted in Egypt ywis.

Let us drink then together by good accord in charity.

I am yours, You are mine, I drink to you, you shall pledge me, if you please.

Hem, ha-hem.

It is good and very fresh, as you would say, in the beginning of the second
degree:

To every one it is not allowed to drink so good,

To every one it is not granted to dwell at Corinthum.

In wine is truth, that is to say, In wine is truth.

Hark my friend, I will tell thee a thing in thine ear, tell no body if thou
love me, it shall rest secret between us two: it is, that I find the wine
better and more pleasant to my taste then I was wont: more then I was wont I
fear the meeting of a bad cup of wine, and to tell you the plain truth, the
odor of wine how much more it is delicious, smirking and surpassing, by so
much more celestial and delicate is it then oil, That is spoken like a man of
learning. I will tell other stories. Tarry a little that I deduce a dram out
of this bottell: Lo here my very and sole Helicon. See here my Fountain
Caballin^. This is mine only Enthusiasmos.

Here drinking, I deliberate, I discourse, I resolve and conclude. After the
conclusion, I laugh, I write, I compose, I drink.

Ennius, the father of Latin Poets, drinking did write. writing did drink.

Aeschylus (if you give credit to Plutarchus in his bankets) did drink
composing, did compose drinking.

Homer never wrote fasting.

Cato never took pen in hand, but after drinking. To the end that you say not
that I live without example of men laudable and best accounted of.

Is there any one that will dispute with me of these intricat problemes of
thirst and drinking: I have no less studied Magic, Negromancie, Alchimie,
the Caballisticke science and Geomancie, then the Philosophy of Hermes
Trismegistus.

These are high matters, and profound sciences.

By our holy Lady, we must be merry: Draw, bring boy, fill wine, ho devil,
pour, I will drink for my part more then five and twenty or thirty three
tuns, before that I die.

Is there no more wine? we are then aground, and in the deserts of Arabia.

It Is vvhot. O how dry I am: As a land without water. I believe that none of
you drinkers doubt of it.

Is there good store of vvines in Gascony this year?

I hope then that we Englishmen shall find infallible remedies against all
alterations and thirsts.

I drink to ye all. You seem to me true Christians: for I drink not to these
dogs the Turks, Mahometains, I deny and renounce them for villains.

The reason why?

I will tell you how these devil Turks are accursed to drink no drop of
wine. If no other mischief were in the Alcoran of Mahound, yet would I
never be of his law.

VVorthie of eternal memory and everlasting praise was the holy man Noe (to
whom we are bounden and greatly beholding for that he planted the vine,
whence flovveth this Angelical, delicious, celestial, ioious, deifying
liquor. (The poor man was deceived in drinking it, for he knew not the virtue
and power thereof.) Have you understood me all this while?

Drink then a good draught without water, for if ye believe it not, here is a
fig for my God-son.

Where are these devil Greeks, who in Alexanders days were renowned
drinkers? O the poor goblins are dead.

We read that these fine Italian demons have drunk well heretofore: and
especially in Julius Caesar's time, when they made their horses, mules, and
mares drink carousse.

I am, believe me a good fellow and a boon companion.

I love to drink neat, and I eat willingly salt meat, and moreover I love to
drink of the best, so does every honest man ywis.

Never noble man hates the good wine.

Drink we, drink we then, as do the camels and dromedaries in the Carauana,
drinking for the thirst past, for the thirst present, and for the thirst to
com^.

So drank Hercules.

Truce of thirst, league of hunger, I am no more angry I thank God and you. I
am gay as a Papingeay, perke as a sparhawke, merry as a butterfly.

Truly it is written by your goodly Euripides, and Silenus the famous
carowsser speaks it.

Mad is the man, and stark out of his wit:
Who drinks carrouse, and laugheth not a whit.
Considering that it is a great while since I was a scholar in Apollos
school, and drank my fill of fount Caballine, among the merry Muses, since
that time I find this nectar divine, this wine precious, this muscadel
delicate.

Gossip fair and softly, your rage in your quick swallowing.

The dill take me, thou have not found thy little sippers of London, who
drink but out of one pipe.

You have a good throat to swallow down.

O fellow mine, if I could mount up as well as I can pour down, I should
already be above the sphere of the Moon with Empedocles.

But I know not what the diue^l this means. This wine is so good and
pleasant. The more I drink, the more thirst I have. I believe the shadow of
these cups, does engender dry mouths, as the moon does merrigalds.

This Liuerots head is good for those that have the gout.

We shall eat few green geese this year.

I had broke my fast well: but therefore will I eat never the less. For I
have a stomach paued and hollow as saint Benets boot.

Ha my friend, give me some pigge.

Diabolo, there is no more liquor, I renounce my life, I die for thirst.

Draw, give here, turn, broile, pour to me without water: so my friend,
firke me this glass finely. Ha false fever vvilt thou not pack hence?

By my fe Gossip I cannot enter into my bets.

You are a cold. my love. I marry, lets speak of drinking. I drink not but at
my hours, as does the Popes mule.

I have great thirst.

Which was first thirst or drinking? Drinking. For Priuatio praesupponit
habitum.

I am a clerk, I tell you, Foecundi calices quem non fecere disertum.


Lets sing, lets drink, lets pour it in, where is my tonnell?

What? I drink but by ae letter of Attornie.

Wet you to dry, or dry you to wet? By my fay I understand not the
Rhetoric. With the pratticque I help my self a little.

Courage, I wet, I moisten, I drink, and all for fear to die, Drink
alvvayes, you shall never die.

If I drink not, I am dead.

For conclusion of mine oration, I will say unto you, that as for me, I think
I am descended of some rich King or prince in old time, For you never saw man,
who had a greater desire to be a King, and rich, then my self to the end to
make good cheer, to take no pains at all, to care for nothing, and to enrich
my friends and all honest and learned men. We have drink enough for one time.

Go to, go to, make a long arm, fear not the Lumbards bit.

We have done well for once truly.

You have made a very sumptuous banquet.

Ye have not touched these roasted capons.

Eat you not of a young phesant Gentlemen?

Granmother do you not love this Lyurots head? What shall we do with these
young Pigeons.

These young suc^ing Rabbets are not the vvorst meat. Taste of this young
Storke. I believe these quails and quailchicks stand against your stomach.

No body will carue up these Partriges, I would they were in the fields and
forests again.

Set here this Sturgion: open this Salmon pie.

Taste these fride gogions.

This sea-crafish, is a most dainty dish.

Ah! fish and flesh at one table.

The sea shall mix it self with the land, the physicians forbid it.

Nay, the physicians will have it so.

I am deceived, I should say that physic forbids it, and not the physicians.

For my part I will speak of fish, I will eat none.

Take away, ho: and bring the fruit.

There Is a marvelous variety in the nature of countries. India (says Virgill)
sends yuorie, and the Sabeans their encens.

There Is a Peach.

See here truly sine apples and delicat pears.

Masters make good cheer, and be merry and frolic.

Serve your selves, for I serve no body.

Lets have some cheese.

I must eat a little cheese.

There is very good.

This is mares cheese.

It is of the milk of a Phrigian or Sicilian shec-asse.

This cheese although it be of England, is full of eyes and worms.

It shallbe good for Flemings then.

This Parmesan is well gathered, and fresher then that Holland cheese, which
is full of eyes also.

It Is not cheese of Parma, but of Placentia.

O that this cheese makes me have a good appetit to drink.

Lets drink then to gain the pardons.

Masters if I had been sur^ of your coming: You have been at too much cost.

The best is, that you be all the very well welcome, and I thank you that you
have taken the pains to come and see me.

Come take away the table: bring here an ewer full of clean water: does it
please you to wash gentles?

Blessed be the good God of heaven for his graces. Much good do you all masters.
Lets have cards and dice to disport our selves here an hour or two by the fire
side.

Pastimes* Cha. 7.
ARe you disposed to be merry and sport the time away alittle?

What sirs! are you all asleep?

Let us go to some game.

Lets go walk.

It Is fine vvalking indeed, the streets are so foul and filthy.

O what fine weather it is to study near the fire side on a pair of new
cards.

I had thought you would have said on some new book.

I say, on a new pair of cards.

Lets rather go to tables.

I could never yet play at tables.

Lets to dice.

I cannot dice it neither.

Lets play at draughts, or at chests then.

No: but we will play at cards.

At cards, be it.

At all plays, there is but the hazard.

I dare not play with you, Tripit-taintie, you are a little too crafty a
companion.

You are a fretter, a vvrangler, a brangler, a foulemouthed villain, and for
nothing you will take exceptions, and quarrel.

You tear, you swear, you forswear.

Thou art a prating rascal, and lie in thy throat in saying so. I play
fairer play then thou do.

By your sleue ye^ we will none of your company: For you have an ill report.

For what? To be a cornifler, a coozener, they say to, that you carry always
with you a bung card in your pocket to coosen simple companions.

Whosoever shall say so, lies in his throat.

Farewell my friend, I would be leath to play in thy company, for thou do
nothing but pick quarrels and vvrangle.

Whiff, my friend, my man is fluced away.

What a companion? you have sent him away with a flea in his ear.

Well, shall we too it now?

But thou Lancelot, so soon as thou have gotten sixpence, you do give them
over, with whom you play.

That is rather to deceive and mock, then to play.

You say true indeed: For if I loose once sixpence, I am tied by the foot till
the Cow come home.

Let us sit down then as we were wont to do, crosse-legged, ho.

Give me this chair, to the end I may loose at more ease.

Shuffle the cards.

Cut, He shall deal who lifts the highest card, I shall deal.

Lets play at Primero.

No, no, we will play at Spanish triumph.

Fie upon it, it is every common alehouse game in England.

At Sant then.

No, for Gods sake, we will play at the Lance-knights game.

Well it is done, cut the cards.

Steven you cut at the boung card.

I know not what it means truly.

There Is for thee Antonie, a dame without blame.

I have a king without a kingdom.

Look this is mine own.

What, master knave of clubs, you are vvelcome.

Will you vvin me six pence?

Here is a ten, a nine, an ace, the four of spades, the three of pickes, the
two of hearts.

A plague of the cards, I shall not turn up one of these cards.

I think that there is no more kings or queens in the deck.

O here is the brother of Robin Hood come.

You have lost the game.

Come six pence for me.

Come a shilling to me.

I Will lay a wager, if I had a hundred crowns in my pursse, I should set them
packing to day at play.

Leave thy prating, if thou vvilt play, stake.

I know if I had a kingdom I should loose it now.

Play no more then.

Yet must I needs play.

Deal the cards with a plague.

What a vengeance, I shall loose as much to day, as my Lord mayor is vvorth.

There Is an eight for you.

I will lay on this eights head, eight crowns. I will then cast up the cards.

Play but six only, and I will hold them.

Play then, Take a card for your self, Thou have a five.

Play fair play and be hanged, look not on the bottom card.

What Is this in the name of God?

God was never plaier at cards.

O fortune happy now be luckie.

One five fingers now, to get me five crowns.

Here it is by God.

This is mine.

Let my money alone companion.

And why?

Go to, go to, I say.

Sblood, you shall have none of my money.

And wherefore?

Therefore, I say.

I will be judged.

Say companion, has he played fair play?

I think so surely.

Did not he take the bottom card?

I will be judged by him.

No truly: he has won the money, and you have lost it.

Take it then.

These cards are marked on the backs,

Thou know them all.

The greatest part of them truly, I know by the faces when I look upon them.

Peradventure also by the backs.

I will cast these cards into the fire.

They shall loose me no more money.

What have you lost all at one cut of cards?

It Is no matter, I will play no more at cards.

I have lost enough for one sitting.

Dicing. Chap. 8.
LEts go to a dicing house to hazard every man his crown.

I have but half a crown to loose.

If I thought to loose, I would not play a farthing.

Give us dice here.

Here are the oxen, the little bones.

Lets play at Gresko.

I will play at passage, or at hazard

What set you companion?

But this one Kar-de-kew.

I have won it.

What, what, we play not yet a good.

I have vvoon it, and I will have it.

You shall not carry away my Kar-de-kew, so Why so?

Because I have lost it but by one ace only.

One ace is enough to loose.

One ace only, lost S. Martin his cloak.

By God you shall pay me for all that.

There Is another matter in it.

And what?

You are a cogger of dice.

These dice are horned, they are false.

They are full of quicksilver, or gold within.

What a villainous thief art thou?

What a rogue is this?

Art not thou a rascal, to coosen men in this manner.

See me these dice run low.

They are highmen, and cut by-ace fashion.

You are a coosening mate.

Go to the Gallows.

Be gone fellow fellon, or I will stob thee through the hand with my poignard.

Come no more here, if thou will believe me, with thy bar-kater-treas.

Go walk to the gallows.

The Tenise-play. Cha. 9.
SHall we play a set at tenise you and I?

Lets go to the great Bracke at White Hall.

Where is the master that keeps the tenis

Here I am sir, what is your pleasure?

Give us some soft and gentle shoes here.

Rackets and bals bring here ho.

Well, play.

I have fifteen.

A loss, mark that chase there.

Fifteen all.

This racket is not vvorth a rush.

Some more rackets ho.

Now give me a fair ball.

I cannot take a ball above hand, nor at rebound.

The chase is mine.

I am thirty.

Thirty all.

Ask standers by, I touched it not.

Forty five.

At dews then.

A ball, I have the advantage.

The set is mine.

I will band a ball more then six score paces mounting, with this racket
which you refuse.

Look here.

O devil! vvat a firking stroke is that.

You have an arm of iron.

Hunting. Chap. 10.
I Like the pastime of hunting above all other sports.

I have a fine grayhound.

And I a bitch that will kill a hare in her form.

There is no pleasure in that.

I love a life to see the beagles hunt and grayhounds run.

You have a fair mastiffe, what can he do?

Lap, bowl, howl, and bark in the morning.

The barking cur is often fearful, and biteth not.

One should for all that believe the barking of an old dog.

We will couple my Grayhound and your mastiffe together.

My mastiffe will not be led in a lease.

It is a hard matter to use an old dog to a s^^ing.

Let us uncouple our beagles, they shall follow the tracke, and trace after the
footing.

The hare is now in her form but in the evening she will come to relief under
this forest.

Thrust your Ferret into the bury.

There are no conies in this warren.

This pasturage is full of the dunging of vvild boares, stags, hinds, and fawns.

Lets discover first the heard.

What a fine pray it is, a conie in a brake to enclose.

We shall catch rather a vvoodcocke at the nose.

Look what a heard of vvild beasts here is.

VVind your horn, let slip your mastif. Bugle your horn, vvind him double,
Set your snares, grins, and purs-nets.

Where are your beagles?

See a goodly stag with ten horns.

Behold here his bracke, lets gallop, lets spur cut to catch him with your
mastiffe.

Let him go, let him run.

He will escape from our dogs well enough, and our horses are almost tired
already.

The Falkener. Chap. 11.
FAlkoner, where is your Gerfalcon.

I have her in my m^w.

Lets go fly at Partridge.

Bait your lure.

What bird is that?

She was sold to me for a falcon.

It Is an owl, or a Gilwhooter.

No sir, it is a Lanard.

She will serve to take birds with a call.

I sweat, I am vvearie. I am very hungry, and all on a water.

I am not hungry, but I am very dry.

I am also vvearie of hunting, and to catch nothing.

VVind thy horn huntsman: blow a blast of thy horn, for I Have lost all my
dogs.

It Is a common proverb:

Of birds, of arms, of dogs and love:

For one pleasure many woes we prove.

The Barber. Chap. 12.
WHat does the gentle Barber?

Welcome, sir.

I come to trim my beard and my hair.

Sit you down there: you shall be trimd by and by.

Will you vvash me, for I have great have.

Stay a little, I have almost done with this gentleman.

Come give me some clean clothes.

What says your Almanacke Barber?

That the moon is just in the eclipse of money.

When is it good to bleed?

When there are any crowns to be gotten.

You are as covetous as the Devil.

I ask nothing else always: but health and a purseful of money, for my
paramour a pretty conie, And Paradise at the end of my days.

You have your beard tangled and knotty.

Undo my locks with this comb.

Rub not so hard.

Rub softly.

A Pomander and some soape, ho.

Hold up this basin.

VVash me gently.

Shall I cut your hair.

Will you have your beard shaven?

Shall I wash your neck, breast and stomach?

Shall I pick your teeth.

Boy, where be my Cizars.

Give me this Iuorie comb.

Sharpen a little the rasor.

Shall I cut your mustaches?

An eare-picker, and a tooth-picker, ho.

You are almost trimmed.

Take the glass and behold your self.

I am well.

Santie dear, I look with a fierce and fellon looks.

There Is to drink, Adieu.

ACheptez quelque liver nouueau, mons. voyla les dernieres nouuelles de France.

Quelz livers acheptez vous?

Tenez, regardez si vous scauriez fournir ce bulletin.

Le Papillon de Belleau: la mousche de Lucian: Ouide de la puce and le noyer,
Ronsard en louange de la fourmi, son liver de la Grenouille and Freslon.
Phauorin des Febvres quartes.

Combien tous ces gros volumes ensemble?

Ne vous faut il pas auoir des autres livers monsieur?

Monstrez moy la Description des Indes Occidentalles, en Espagnol. Que ie voye
la premiere and second sepmaine du Bartas en Francois. Les Oeuures de
Petrarque, and les Iournees du Iean Boccace en Italien: les Commentaires du
Iulles Caesar en Latin: l'Histoire d'Heliodore en grec: le nouueau Testament en
la Syriacque: l'Alchoran des Turcs en l'Arabesque, and le Thalmud des Iuifs en
Hebrieu.

Les voulez vous de l'impression de Lion ou de Paris?

Imprimez a Basle ou a Venise, si voulez. Iem'en vay voir par la cemitiere si
ie les peux trouuer.

Il faut que ie les aye necessairement pour mon retour a l'Vniuersite. ai

Homme de bien quel liver demandez vous?

Il me faut achepter un certain liver, mais il ne m' en souuient pas du nom.

Est-ce en verse ou en prose? Non non, c'est vne histoire. N'auez vous pas
quelque ioly. petit liver pour lyre au coing de la cheminee?

Voyla les sept sages de Greece: and voyla les sept sages maistres de Romme: and
voyci les sept Sages de Gotham qui noyerent l'anguille en la mer.

C'est cestuy la mesme que ie cerche.

Il est mignonnement rely en veau, mons.

C'est bien dit friar Tibaut.

Combien, combien, and qu'ayons qu'vn mot.

Ie n'ayme pomt de barguigner: prenez le pour un sol.

Ma foy ie vous en donneray trois beaux deniers.

Vne maille dauantage, c'est a vous.

Pas un liard, prenez l'argent si voulez.

Bien bien venez-ca, reuenez vne autre fois: puisque c'est a vous, vous ne
l'aurez pas asteure, vous di-ie:

Vous vous mocquez de moy donc: adieu.

Ie voudrois veoir un bon velours noir.

Voyla vne piece de fort bonne estoffe.

Est-ce de trois poils ou de deux? N'est il pas gomme?

Non ie vous en asseure sur ma foy.

De quel prix est il? de vingt and trois solz la verge.

I'en ay bien que ie vous vendray a dix huict, mais il n'est pas fi bon que
cestuy-ci.

Monstrez moy vne piece de satin tanne.

Voyla un satin plein de soye. Que vous en semble?

N'est il pas bon and de belle couleur?

La couleur est fraische and belle: toutesfois i'en ay veu du meilleur satin.

Ie le croy a grand'peine.

Monstrez moy quelque autre couleur plus vifue.

Ie vous en monstreray de toutes couleurs, de toutes sorts and a tous prix que
voudriez.

Que demandez vous madamoyselle? Que cerchez vous seigneur, mons. homme de
bien? Venez-ca.

Escoutez mon amy, ie vous voudrois dire un mot a l'aureille: me voulez vous
fire le satin d'vn pourpoint, and du velours pour me fair un haut de chausses?

Que dites vous? parlez plus haut. Ie ne vous entends pas asteure. Me voulez
vous fair credit and fire?

Fi-fi, le fire engendre fiebure.

Est donc l'amitie si froide enter nous deux?

L'amour fait beaucoup: and l'argent fait tout.

Adieu, donc, ie me recommende.

Le Peintre. Chap. 5.
DIeu te guard Peintre.

Bien venuz mes gentilz compagnons.

Quelle pourtraicture est cela que tu peignez la? Du sage Aesope.

O quelles leures, and narines de singe il a.

Il est oreille comme un Asne.

Que dites-vous de ceste figure?

Ie die que si c'est Venus, elle n'a pas le visage bien peint. C'est un
Harlequin Italien.

Il est, croyez moy, tresbien contrefaict pour un sot.

Il n'est pas trop bien pourtrait pour un sage.

Qu'y a il a dire?

Il est bossu and tortu, and a le nez aquilin.

Les Perses adoroyent ceux qui avoyent le nez aquilin, a cause de Cyrus, lequel
ilz disent auoir eu le sien de form d'un chausse-pied.

Il a les mains fort crochues and glueuses.

Il n'est pas toutes-fois larron.

Que scait-il fair?

Il scait taire and tenir son secret.

Il est donc plus sage que plusieurs sots.

De quoy a cest homasse la face si rouge and enflambee?

Honte le saisit auec horreur des meschancetez, abominations and vilainies
commises par le monde, ou plustost de veoir les aueugles iuger de couleurs.

virtue Dieu! quels merchants?

Mais nous en voulons achepter, moyennant que tu nous laisses fair le prix a
nostre fantasy.

Voy la un beau march. Il y a long temps que ie suis embrouille de vous.

Ostez vous d'icy, ie vous en pry.

Nous ne faisons point de mal.

Quelle image est cela?

C'est l'image de la vierge MARIE.

Est-ce pas nostre Seigneur IESV CHRIST?

Voire d'vne pomme pourrie donc.

Ie suis fort ignorant de tell abus.

Voyez vous ceste Mappe-Monde? Ie la voy bien.

Qu'est-ce, vne mer? Ie n'y voy qu'vn peu d'eaue, qui n'est plus grande que la
Thamise.

Ie m'ebais que le Roy de France ne fait pas fair un beau pont pour passer
d'vn pais a l'autre.

La mer enter la France and l'Angleterre n'est elle plus large que cela? Ma-foy
ie tyreray vne flesche donc aysement du Chasteau de Doure iusques aux arenes
blanches de Calais.

Regardez icy la grand mer Oceane.

Est-ce la mer Oceane, Cela? Est celle-cy la terre?

On dit que le Sieur Francois Draec, le Seigneur Candich, and Magellanes ont
este quasi trois ans a enuironner and circuir la Terre and la Mer, Par may foy,
pour gaigner vne bonne pint du vin claret, en moins d'vne iournee, en un bell
apresdisne, ie les circuiray bien a beau pied sans cheual, asne, mule, nauire,
basteau ou fuste.

Tes febures quartaines, tu n'en feras rien.

Regard icy Couillon! Voy l'Asie. Icy sont Tygris and Euphrates. Voyci Quinzay
ville tant renommee enter les Asiatiques: and a douze mill ponts de pierre

DIeu y soit. Dormez vous?

Un mal vient rarement seul.

O que la test me fait aussi grand mal.

C'est donc le mal de Naples.

O qui me deliurera de mes douleurs?

Attendez i'ay icy un cemittre qui fera l'office.

Qui ont este voz medecins?

Les plus experts du monde es Vrines: Car ils m'ont mis de mal sepmaine en mal
an, and m'ont change un chancre Francois en un biscancaro Napolitain.

Vous estes vous fait saigner?

Le Barbier m'a tire trois onces de sang de la vein capitalle, and le medecin
dix de la vein crumenalle, and m'a ordonne un Recipe Pullos Septem, etc.

Que dist-il de vostre mal?

Ie dit que c'est le mal d'Italie, de France, d'Espaigne, d'Allemaigne and
d'Angleterre.

Quel mal est cela?

Le mal Catholic, le mal commun, le gran mal.

Ne scauez vous pas autre nom?

On l'appelle la verolle en France: Ie ne scay comment on l'appelle en
Angleterre.

Parlez bas, voycy Monsieur le Docteur.

Mon amy ouurez la bourse: ie die la bouche. Marchez un peu.

pillules demain au matin and vous vous porterez bien par la grace de Dieu.

Iaymeroye plustost manger vne merde que de mascher and aualler ces pillules,
tant else sont ameres.

Et moy i'aymeroye plustot boire du vin que de l'eau.

On les en mange bien en France and en Italy, mais en Angleterre on n'en tient
pas conte.

Ho friar Iean, ne mangez vous pas de ce bon poisson?

Pour vous en dire la rayson, il y a tell saison, que le poisson est poison.

Tastez de ceste truite: humez de ce broued d'anguilles, cela vous eschauffera
les boyaux, and les lauera doucement pour fair bon ventre.

De tous oyseaux ie n'ayme point l'oye ny l'oyson.

De tous poissons i'estime l'anguille poison.

De tous poissons frais fors la tenche, prens l'aisle de la perdrix, ou la
cuisse d'vne nonnain.

Ie aim fort le blanc d'vn chapon. En cela vous ne ressemblez point aux
Renards, car des chapons, poules, and pouletz qu'ils prennent, iamais ne
mangent ilz le blanc. Pourquoy? pource qu'ilz n'ont point des cruisiniers a les
cuire.

Lacquay du vin, versez a moy. A vous mon host, a vous mon hostess, ie vous y
feray rayson icy.

Ie croy qu'il gele, il fait si grand f^oid Leuons nous messires and approchons
nous du feu.

Le bon Dieu de Parady,
Soit pour tous ces biens beny.

Le coucher. Chap. 18.
IE me sens un peu mal dispos apres souper.

L'estomac me fait mal.

Mon host ie me veux aller reposer. I'ay grand envy de dormir. Le sommeil
m'est tomb des-ia dedans mes yeux. Qu'on me monstre ma chamber.

Quand vous voulez, la fille vous y monstrera le chemin. Bon soir mon host,
bon soir bon-nuict mon hostess. Dieu vous doint bonne nuict and bon repos a
trestous.

Il vous faut monter par icy monsieur. Voicy vostre chamber, voicy vostre lict,
voyla les priuez, and voyci un pot de chamber.

Ridez les courtines. Prestez moy un couure-chef ou vne coiffe. I'ay un bonnet
de nuict en mon sein.

Voz linceux sont netz.

Regardez, qu'ilz soient bien secs, ie vous pry.

Ie les ay seiche au pres du feu.

Tirez mes chausses* Couurez moy de ma rob.

I'ay peu de couerture. Donnez moy un autre oreiller, ie ne puis coucher si bas.

Estes vous bien asteure? en voulez vous encore de couuerture? Ne vous plaist
il autre chose?

Nenny pour le present. Dormezbien.

Escoutez Gaudinette, baisez moy vne fois mamie, deuant que vous en allez.

Baisez moy, and ie vous rendray voz braceletz, que ie vous ostay l'autre iour
en iouant auec vous.

Ne me parlez plus, ie vous en pry de baiser n'y d'amour, mais rendez moy mes
bracelets, car autrement que me diront mon pere and ma mere? Ils se
courrouceront a moy.

Non feront, non feront.

BOn iour ait vostre Seig. Seigneur Vespasien.

Ma foy les amiss se rencontrent plus souuent que montaignes.

L'amour pass le gant and l'eau le houseau.

Vous ne tenez plus conte de petits compagnons.

Non, non, ie ne vis sinon auec les Princes and enter les grands Seigneurs.

D'ou vient la Seigneurie vostre?

Ie viens de veoir les dix-sept terres du pais bas.

Vous estes deuenu trop rich.

Philosophalle qui m'attire l'argent des bourses comme l'aymant attire te fer.

I'enrage Diables, i'enrage, tenez moy, Diables, tenez moy.

Vrayement Hercules n'est rien a vous, qui estant au berceau tua les deux

Il vous feroit chier vinaigre deuant ^out le monde.

Ie le feray boire d'autant. A gens de guerre ne faut bon vin espargne^.

Vous contez sans vostre host.

I'ay grand peur que devant qu'il soit nuict, on vous estrillera a grand coup
de Musquette and d'Arquebouse.

Hon! que ie ne suis roy de France pour quatre vingts dix ou cent ans: par Dieu
ie vous mettrois en chien courtaut messieurs les fuyarts de Pauie.

Ie hay plus que poison un homme qui fuist quand il faut iouer des cousteaux.

Ie crains la mort non plus qu'vn Papillon, ou la morsure d'vne pulce a
l'aureille: and quant a moy ie ne crains pas de combattre a vne armee entiere,
si ce n'est de ces mescreants Tartres, Cannibales, Indiens, and Moscouites, qui
tirent a tort a trauers, deca de la, par cy par la, de long de large, dessus
dessoubs.

A la verite les prouesses de Camille, Scipion, Pompee, Caesar and Themistocles
ne sont pas si grandes que les vostres.

Ie tue les gens comme Diomedes tuoit les Thraces, and comme Vlyxes ie mets les
corpse es pieds de mes cheuaux, ainsi que raconte Homere.

Ie suis pourtant d'aduis que si les ennemis se rendent que les y preniez a
mercy and rencon. Car clemence est vne vraye virtue royale.

Ho mord boeuf, sang boeuf. Par la virtue mon amy si ie ne boute tout a feu and
a sang ie renie ma vie.

Vous m'effrayez a ainsi iurer.

Tu es vne best de la montagne.

Mon amy ie choque si rudement sur les ennemis, que ie les renuerse comme

L'artillerie commence a foudroyer les murailles.

L'alarme commence a s'eschauffer. Les trompettes sonnent a la retraicte.

Ou es tu Line tresdoux musicien auec ton luc d'luoire? Ou es tu Amphion
tresdocte harpeur de la Greece? ou es tu Orpheus, auec ton cystre d'argent? ou
es tu Arion auec ta musicque qui feist dancer les poissons en la mer? Venezca,
apprenez icy des lecons nouuelles. Certes ces quatre qui sont celebrez en tous
autheurs Grecs and Latins pour auoir este tresexcellents musiciens n'ont iamais
sceu contrefaire ceste petite musicienne.

Escoutez, voyla bien un autre oiseau qui chante as^eure.

C'est un merle ou un Rossignol.

Le Rossignol ne chante que du soir and matin.

Ou est il, ie vous pry?

C'est un Rossignol ie l'entends bien.

Ne le voy tu pas pose sur un rameau?

O qu'il chante melodieusement: sans se donner relasche: and ne cesse point!

Il ne se faut point esmerueiller s'il chante doucement, veu qu'il est
d'Athenes, ou les vagues de la mer flottent aussi contre les riuages par mesure.

Pline escrit qu'il chante deuant les hommes plus longuement and plus
soigneusement.

Qu'en est la cause? Ie te la deceleray.

Le Coucou and le Rossignol chantent en mesme saison de l'an, ascauoir, au
printemps depuis la mi-Auril iusqu'a la fin de May ou enuiron. Ces deux oiseaux
vindrent en different de la douceur de leur chant, on cherche un judge, and par
ce que la dispute estoit du son, l'asne fut trouue fort commode a ceste
cognoissance, lequel plus que toutes les autres bestes a les oreilles grandes.
L'asne ayant repousse le Rossignol, duquel il disoit n' entendre la harmony,
adiugea la victoire au coucou, le Rossignol en appella par deuant l'homme,
lequel quand il le voit, incontinent il meine sa cause, and chante diligemment,
a fin de fair sa cause bonne pour se venger du tort que l'asne luy avoit fait.

Qui est l'homme au monde (dit il) tant grossier, lourd stupide ou hebete, qui
ne s'estonne, and qui ne soit raui d'vne incroyable delectation, escoutant la
melody, qui sort du Rossignol, and comme vne voix si hautaine and harmonieuse
peut issir d'vn si petit tuyau? Outre qu'il persevere si obstinement en son
chant que la vie luy defaudra aussi tossed que la voix: de sort qu'il semble,
qu'il ait este instruit de quelque maistre a chanter en music, car il
contrefait tantost le bas, tantost le haut, tantost la taille, tantost le
dessus, and apres qu'il est bien ennuye de gringoter il contrefait sa voix, and
iugeriez que c'est un autre oiseau qui ne chante plus que le plain chant: puis
tout en un coup il penetre si haut, qu'il se passionne, se pasme, and demeure
quasi extatique par vne infinite de melodieux passages, qui rauissent l'ame
iusques au ciel, non seulement des hommes, mais des autres petits oiselets,
lesquels il charm and arreste de son chant, and les conuie par sa douceur a
l'escouter and tascher a le contrefaire, and luy desrober quelque chose de sa
melody. Et non content de cela vous le verrez instruire ses petits, les
prouoquer a semblable harmony, leur apprenant tantost a obseruer leur tons, a
les conduire d'vne mesme haleine, les vns en longueur aspirer, les autres

C'est vne chose merueilleuse de la Nature de cest animal.

Contez nous en quelque chose gentil babillard.

C'est vne chose presque monstreuse en nature de la nature de ces Araignees, de

fair leur toils, and les pescheurs a fair leur rets. Mais else ont beaucoup
meilleure grace and plus grand advantage en leurs industries: La femme and la
fille font les toiles and filetz, and le mary chasse d'autre part pour leur
nourriture, and est aux aguetz cache dans quelque trou en la muraille pour
attendre and surprendre vne best pour la fair tomber en ses retz: and encore
que son corpse ne soit gueres plus gros qu'vn pois, il a toutefois tant de
viuacite and industry qu'il prend quelque fois de grosses mousches and petits
lezars en ses retz, and si observe si bien la saison de chasser qu'il semble
estre astrologue: il est au contraire de nous qui attendons le beau temps: mais
il chasse quand le temps est nubileux, qui nous est un presage de pluye selon
les deux grands Philosophes Aristote and Pline.

C'est, comme dit Platon, au second liver de sa Republique la best du monde
plus Philosophe.

Mais veistes vous onques un chien rencontrant quelque os medullaire: si veu
l'auez, vous auez peu noter de quelle devotion il le guette, de quel soing il
le guard: de quelle ferueur il le tient, de quelle police il met ses pieds la
dessus, de quelle prudence il l'entamme, de quelle affection il le brise, and
de quelle diligence il le succe.

Qui l'a induit a ce fair? Quel est l'espoir de son estude? Quel bien pretend
il?

beaucoup de toutes autres.

de nature, comme dit Galenus 3. lib. fac. Not. and 11. de vsu Partium

De toutes les bestes il n'y a pas vne plus sage ny plus subtle que le singe.

O la meschante best. Il ne fait que s'en mocquer de tout le monde: and montre
le cul a tous venans: Il ne guard point la mayson comme un chien: il ne tire
pas la charrue comme le boeuf: il ne produist ny laict ny lain comme la
brebis: il ne port pas son maistre comme le cheual: il ne port pas de faix

pourquoy de tous il recoit tant des bastonnades and mocqueries.

cestuyla qui ioua a la fossette auec nous, fait estat de merchandise, ayant

Qu'est ce que i'oy.

Ce Hierosme vous l'auez bien cognu gros and gras, d'vn visage vermeil, en bon
point, ioyeux, d'vne face luysante, plaisant, raillard: maintenant il est
maigre, descolore, d'vne pasle couleur and comme meurdrie, foible, hideux,

null d'iceux qui l'auoit veu auparauant ne le scauroit recognoistre.

O le pouure and miserable ieune homme! D'ou luy vient son mal?

D'amour. D'amour? Dites vous?

Maintenant il est enrage: il est fol: souuentesfois il se pourmeine tout seul:
mais tousiours sans parler, tousiours ou chantrouillant ou gringotant quelque
chose, and escrit des carmes en vulgaire a son amoureuse.

O chetif garcon!

Maintenant vous le verrez feindre vne mer des larmes, un lac des miseres,
redoubler ses cris, accuser le ciel, mauldire a la terre, fair vne anatomy de
son coeur, geler, ardre, adorer, idolatrer, admirer, feindre des paradis,
forger des Enfers, fair le Sisiphe, iouer l^ Tantale, contrefaire le Titie.
Tout quant and quant il exalte en ses verse celle Diane qu'il aim: ses cheueux
ne sont autre que fill d'or, ses sourcils arches and voutes d'Ebene, ses yeulx
astres iumeaux, ses regards esclairs, sa bouche coral, ses dents perles
d'Orient, son aleine basme, ambre, musc, sa gorge de neige, son col de laict,
ses mammelles qu'elle a sur l'estomac montagnes ou pommes d'Alabastre.

Tout le rest du corpse n'est plus qu'vne prodigalite and thresoir du ciel and
de la nature qu'elle auoit reserve pour co^bler de toute parfection sa
maistresse and bien-aimee.

Ie les veux lyre hautement: Escoutez:

Madamoyselle, vostre beauty est tant excellent, tant singuliere, tant
celeste, que ie croy que Nature l'a mise en vous comme un paragon pour nous
donner entendre combien elle peust fair, quand elle veut employer toute sa
puissance and tout son scauoir. Ce n'est que miel, ce n'est que succre, ce
n'est que manne celeste de tout ce qu'est en vous. C'estoit a vous a qui Paris
deuoit adiuger la pomme d'or, non a Venus, non, ny a Juno, ny a Minerue, car
oncques n'y eut tant de magnificence en Juno, tant de prudence en Minerue, tant

heureux sera celuy a qui ferez celle grace de vous accoller, de vous baiser,
and de coucher auec vous: Ie ne scay si ie suis predestine des Phees: parquoy
ie me reccommends a vostre bonne grace and vous baisant bien humblement les
blanches mains, ie vous dis, Adieu sans Adieu.

Les Alquemistes ne sont pas faux-monnoyeurs.

Non, mais les sophisticateurs des mettaulx sont faux compagnons.

Ie ne die ne bien ne mal d'eux, qu'ilz multiplient tant qu'ilz veuillent.

Vois-tu ceste Croix?

C'est un des plus beaux monuments de la Ville de Londres.

Nous nous sommes rendus a la Bourse asteure.

La Bourse fut bastie l'an 1567 par le Seigneur Thomas Gressam; qui donna aussi
