La louange de France traduite des oeuures de Guillaume de Saluste Seigneur du
Bartas.
O Mill and mill fois terre heureuse and ^econde!
O perle de l' Europe! o Paradis du Monde!
France ie te salve, o mere des guerriers,
Qui iadis ont plant leurs triumphans lauriers
Sur les riues d' Euphrate, and sanglante leur glaiue
Ou la torche du iour and se couche and se leave:
Mere de tant d'ouuriers, qui d'vn hardi bon heur
Taschent comme obscurcir de Nature l'honneur:
Mere de tant d'esprits qui de scauoir espuisent
Aegypte, Greece, Rome, and sur les doctes luysent
Comme un iaune esclattant sur les palles couleurs,
Sur les astres Phoebus, and sa fleur sur les fleurs.
Tes fleuues sont des mers, des Provinces tres Villes,
Orgueilleuses en murs, non moins qu'en moeurs ciuiles.
Ton terroir est fertile, and temperez tes airs:
Tu as pour Bastions and deux monts, and deux mers.
Le Crocodile fire tes riuages n'infeste,
Des piolez Serpents la race porte-peste
Sur le verd de tes fleurs a rompu-dos rempant,
N'aulne de sa longueur la longueur d'vn arpent.
Le Tigre aux pieds volants ne fait ses brigandages
Dans tes monts cauerneux, le Lion ses carnages
Dans tes bruslants deserts: and le Cheual de l'eau
Ne train tes enfans soubs un vagueux tombeau.
Que si le rich flot de tes fleuues ne roll
L'or auec ses caillous: si de tes monts ne coule
Un argent espure: si nous ny trouuons pas
Le Grenat, le Ruby, la Perle a chaque pas:
Tes Toiles, ton Pastel, tes Laines, tes Salines,
Ton Froment and ton Vin, sont d'assez riches mines
Pour te fair nommer Reine de l'Vniuers.
La se^le paix te manque. O Dieu qui tiens ouuers
Tousiours les yeux sur nous, de l'eau de ta Clemence
Amorti le brasier qui consume la France:
Balaye nostre ciel: Remets o Pere doux,
Remets dans ton carquois les traicts de ton courroux.
FINIS.
THE SECOND METHOD OF the true and natural prononciation of the French,
follows dilated in xx. chapters, by the most difficult French letters and
vovvels, which are set in the margin, with their English value just against
the familiar talk of Trades, very delectable to read. The Booke-seller. Chap.
1.
BVy some new book sir, there are the last news from France.

What books buy you?

Hold see if you can furnish me this note.

The Butterfly of Bellauius. The fly of Lucian. Ovid of the Flea and the
Nut-tree. Ronsard in praise of the Ant his book of the Frog, and of the Waspe.
Phauorin of fevers quartane.

How all these great volumes together?

Want you no other books sir?

Show me the description of the West Indies in Spanish. Let me see the first
and second week of Bartas in French. The works of Petrarque, and the
Iornataes of John Boccace in Italian: the Commentaries of Julius Caesar in
Latin. The history of Heliodore in Greek. The new Testament in the Assirian
tongue: the Alchoran of the Turks in the Arabian: and the Thalmud of the Jews
in Hebrew.

Will you have them of Lions or Paris print? imprinted at Basil or at Venice,
if you will? I go to see in the Churchyard if I can find them.

I must needs have them against my return to the University.

Honest man what book lack you?

I must buy a certain book but I cannot hit of the name of it.

Is it in verse or in prose? No no, it is a history. Have you not some pretty
little book to read in the chimnie corner?

There are the seven sages of Greece, and there are the seven wise masters of
Rome: and here are the seven wise men of Gotham, who drownded the Eele in the
sea.

That is the very same that I seek for.

It is finely bound in Calfes leather sir.

Well said brother Tibald.

What Is price, how, and let us have but one word.

I love not to make many words, take it for a shilling.

By my fay I will give you a fair piece of three pence.

One half penny more it is yours.

Not a farthing, take money if you will.

Well, well, come hither, come again another time, seeing that it is to you,
you shall go without it now, I tell you.

You mock me then, Adieu.

The Mercer. Chap. 2.
WHat lack you sir? I have here good wrought velvet of Geanes, Sattins of
Lucques and of Cypres, Chamblet without waves, cloth of gold, cloth of silver,
damask for damsels, Spanish taffataes, Millan fustians, Worsteds of Norwich.

I would see a good black velvet.

There is a piece of very good stuff.

Is it three piled or two piled? Is it not gummed?

No I assure you of my faith.

Of what price is it? Of 23. shillings the yard.

I have some that I will sell you for eighteen, but it is not so good as this.

Show me a piece of tawnie satin.

There is a satin full of silk. What say you?

Is it not good and of a fair color?

The color is fresh and fair: yet I have seen a better satin.

I think hard and skant.

Show me some other more lively color.

I will show you of all colors, of all sorts, and of all prices that you will.

What lack you Gentlewoman? what seek you, Gentleman, sir, honest man, come
hither.

Hark my friend, I would speak a word in your ear: Will you trust me the
satin of a doublet, and velvet to make me a pair of breeches?

What say you? speak higher. I cannot understand you now. Will you trust me
and give me credit?

Fy-fi, trusting engendereth the Fever.

Is then between us two the love so small?

Love does much, and money does all.

Farewell then, I commend me to you.

The Goldsmith. Chap. 3.
What lack ye fair maiden?

Show me a gylden salt, a carved goblet, a cup finely garnished.

There is fair plate, very rich vessel: most fair stuff well burnished, and
exceeding rich.

My love, shall I show you a fair jewel, a ring, with a diamond enchased in
it?

Is it weighty?

It vvaigheth a crown and half in gold.

What is the price good sir?

At few words eight crowns.

I dare not give so much.

Say sir, what shall I pay for this jewel to hang in the ear?

This pendent my love is of pearl wrought in manner of a vine: at a word two
crowns.

You ask too much a great deal.

What offer you then?

That which I told you.

We are far from bargaining.

One Swallow makes not the spring.

nor one only merchant the market.

I had rather have a black pendent of Iett after the manner of France.

Show me a Carkenet.

Sir, I am a Goldsmith, I sell no Jewels.

Pardon me if you please, I took you for a distiller of milstones.

I ask for a Lapidaries shop.

Go to the sign of the Hunters horn.

God save you sir of the Horn.

Welcome mistress of the Cornet.

Have ye not some fair Topase set in gold?

No but I have a very fair Turquois.

Is it Oriental?

I recovered it of late beyond the country of the Iapans in the Northren Asia.

Where in China?

Yea mary not far from thence in Quinzay the imperial State among the Chinos.

Is it possible? Let me see a touchstone.

Lo there is a fair Emerawde.

This Diamond where was it cut?

It has the points very sharp, and was cut in Cairo or in Canaria, at Venize
or in Alexandria.

How sell you that chain of gold?

For three hundred crowns.

How many linkes contains it?

Some five hundred and fifty.

Did you never see any of these little rings at London, that they sell at
Venice, in the Collets whereof, is set under a fine Crystal a little Scorpion
of iron wagging the tail very artificially.

I have been in Italy and have seen many of the same making.

Will you make me such a one?

Yea: for ten crowns.

Fail not then against to morrow when I shall come again this way. Adue.

The Painter. Chap. 5.
GOd bless you Painter.

Welcome my fine laddes.

What portraicture is that thou paintest there? Of Esope the sage.

O what lips and no sethrils of an Ape he has!

He is eared like an Ass.

What say you of this figure?

I say that if it be Venus, she has not her face well painted. It is an
Italian Harlekin.

He is believe me, very well counterfeited for a fool.

He is not very well shadowed for a wise man.

What wants there?

He is crump-shouldered and crooked, and has a Hawkesnose.

The Persians adored those who had an Aquiline nose, for Cyrus sake, who they
say, had his of form like a shooing-horne.

He has his hands very crooked and limy fingerd, For all that he is not a
thief.

What can he do?

He can hold his peace and keep his own counsel.

He is then wiser then many fools.

Wherefore has this dwarf his face so red and fiery?

The shame and horror seaseth him of the villainies, wickedness and
abominations committed by the world, or rather to see the blind judge of
colors.

Be gone hence, for you will buy nothing, I see well, pack, pack: Gods Lord!
what merchants!

But we will buy so that thou will let us make the price at our pleasure.

There is a fair bargain. It Is a long while that I am troubled with you.

Be gone hence I pray you.

We do no harm, What image is that?

It Is the image of the Virgin MARIE.

Set me down this Table.

Is not this our Lord JESUS CHRIST?

Who is portraited and pictured so lively here?

It Is the Roman T. Caesar of famous memory.

The image of an exceeding passing workmanship.

We find written, that Tyberius Caesar had the joints of his fingers so strong,
that with one finger he did pierce through a green Apple.

So also can I.

I through a rotten Apple then.

He has the table of his hand very large.

Have thou learned Chiromancie?

I have heard it spoken of. What then?

Thou should have diuined here some thing by the lines of his hands.

I am very ignorant of such abuse.

See you this Map of the world? I see it well.

What Is this a Sea? I see but a little water, which is not greater then the
Thames.

I marvel the French King does not make a fine bridge to pass from one
country to another.

The Sea between France and England is it no broader then that? By my faith I
will then easily shoot an arrow from Dover Castle to the white sands of Cales.

See here the huge Ocean Sea.

Is that the Ocean Sea? That, Is this the earth?

They say that sir Francis Drake, master Candish, and Magellanes, have been
almost three years in compassing the earth and the Sea.

By my troth to win a good pint of claret wine, in less then a days iourny,
in a fair afternoon, I will go round about them on foot without Horse, Ass,
Mule, Ship-boat or Brygandine.

Thy fevers quartanes thou will not.

Look here Cullion! See Asia. Here are Tygris and Euphrates. See here Quinzay,
a City so famous amongst the Azians: and has xij. thousand stonebridges,
under which the ships pass with full sail, and never pull down their masts.

See Afric! Here is the mountayn of the Moon!

See thou the Fennes of Nyle? Lo here the red Sea.

Look upon the great Caire! On this side is Europe.

This top here all white, are the Hyperborean mountains. Here are the Alps,
over which we go down into Italy. There are the Appenines: and here are the
Pyrenaean hills, by which you may go directly into Spain.

See here where is the City of London. See Powles steeple, You do naught but
soil and foul my merchandise, and will buy nothing I see well.

Show me some fine pretty Tablet to hang in my counting house.

There is one which is a fine piece of work, painted and drawn out of the
work, long since wrought by Ph^lomela, setting forth and showing her sister
Progne, how her brother in law Tereus had defloured her, and cut out her
tongue, to the end that she should not tell.

It Is a most gallant and wonderful sumptuous piece.

This likes me not, show me another.

See here lively painted the Id^es of Plato.

See here the Atoms of Epicurus.

See there Echo after her nature represented.

Behold there the life and gests of Achilles in seventy and eight pieces of
Tapistrie with broad lists, long full four, broad three fathoms, all of
Phrygian silk, embrodered with gold and silver.

Where is the beginning?

The Tapistrie begins at the marriage of Peleus and Thetis, going on with the
nativity of Achilles, his youth written by Stacius Papinius, his gests and
feats of arms celebrated by Homer: his death and burial pend by Ovid, and
Quint Calabrian, ending in the apparition of his shadow, and the sacrifice of
Polixena written by Euripides.

How much? what is the price I pray you?

Ten thousand crowns.

It Is too dear for me that.

What Is this?

It Is the shape of a man that lacks a master.

He has his face lively painted.

He is drawn truly just in all qualities, fit in all gesture, behavior,
looks, gate, phisiognomie and affections.

How much shall I give you for this same Table?

Four Crowns.

Hold, you shall have no more nor less: I will pay you in lack-an-apes pence,
or in fair crowns of the Palace.

Away, away, march I say down, you mock me, I see well enough.

Have you not prated and tatled enough yet? away from hence I say.

Adieu, adieu, Painter adieu.

The Armorer. Chap. 6.
WHere shall I find a two hand-sword?

What will you kill Iacke-a-Lent?

I kill no body, but only that I will stand upon my guard.

What, have you any secret enemies?

I cannot march between eleven and twelve a clock at night, but master
Constable of our ward sends me on a message to the Counter in the Poultrie.

What to do? to buy hens?

By my faith to pay my ransom.

I give to the devil, if I meet him, I will make him an honest man.

Show me a two-edged sword, if you will.

There Is an old blade that has sent many souls to God or the Dill.

This sword is truly too dangerous for a man that's all choler, as I, for it is
to keen, empoysoned, and a shauer.

It has a naughty hilt.

The sheath is worth nothing.

For how much would you sell it?

For four fair crowns.

You ask too much a great deal.

If a thief had stolen it it is worth more.

I see well you will not sell it.

I had rather sell it then give it.

I believe you well.

I assure you it is a Vienna blade in Dalphine.

Let me see it* I Will lay a wager it is a falcion of Ferrara, a Tucke of Toledo, a
Whineyard of Scotland or a Bilbow blade.

Thou have hit the nail on the head, go.

How much this Turkish cem^torie?

Fifty crowns.

Now I know the price, you ask fifty crowns, I will give you five.

Well it is yours then.

A man has no sooner spoken but he is taken at his word.

My friend men take men by their words, and birds by a call.

Why have you bought this Tranchant?

To fear my foes and to fend my friends.

One should not put a sword into the hand of a child, of a fool, of a
drunkard, of a thief, or of a villain.

The Apoticarie. Chap. 7.
POticarie, have you made my drink?

Who prescribed you this receipt?

It Is master Doctor.

What Doctor?

Will you know? Know you not the hand?

No truly.

Albertus Magnus is the author, I have translated it out of his works of the
secrets of damsels.

Do you believe this monstrous liar?

Is he so great a liar?

He says that there is virtue in stones, in herbs, and in words, to make men
in love with women, and women with men. No, no, it is another thing that I
will do.

I will conjure a spirit, and will go invisible.

Let me see your Receipt. Read it.

Take a Frogs tongue, and the blood of a bat.

And how must I use them? beat them well together in a mortar.

Does it bind or loose?

Yea, yea, and make a man go to the etc. lustily.

Take then a violl and stop it well.

What Is that within that box there?

It Is pepper or Ginger.

What have you within this great sack?

They are cloves, nutmegs, saffron, cynnamon and almonds.

What fine drogues are within these boxes there bepainted with shapes of
Harpies, of hares, of flying horses and flying hearts? There is within them,
balm, ambre, amomum, musk, ciuet, perles, and other precious drugs.

Have you no preservative against the disease? you know what I mean.

Lay an emplaister to it.

You need no other Treacle for that.

I dare not purge, for the time is not good.

Have you a hard belly?

I am always bound in my belly almost, bring me a glister to morrow morning.

I understand you well now, let me alone.

Farewell till to morrow morning.

The Horseman. Chap 8.
SEE you that fine horesman there? he is a Prauncer of Ferrara.

He is an Italian gentleman, and rides better a girl then a gelding.

He is mounted for all that on a brave nag, on a gennet, on a barded horse.

How like you his horse, is he not a fine courser.

I tell you sir that he is a Hungarian or Turkish horse.

By your leave it is a great horse of the country of Frize, or of Denmark.

I will lay a wager that it is a courser of Naples, of Mantua or of Ferrara.

For a quart of wine it is a Barbarian horse, or a Gennet of Spain.

He trotteth marvelous well. See, see he falls to his amble again. He is of
a most fierce courage and proud.

He is very young. He is but yet a colt. He is fat and well liking. He is
covered with a fair Capparison.

That helps nothing to the goodness of the horse.

He has great and fair eyes, and plaieth without cease with his bit
froathing and foming.

He shows that the bit is not his master.

Ah! what a mincing pace he has? He is quick in managing. See you him go one
while overthwart, now on the left side, then on the right, and touches the
ground but a very little, with the tip of his hoof only?

O that this light horse fetches fine friskes, he is as a light as a feather,
and runs very swift.

O that he raineth well, how he frounceth his neck, carrying his head aloft
and his ear upright.

See how he moves with fierceness and heat his brows, and trampleth with
all his members bravely. Behold how the sitter makes him fluce in the air,
leap the ditch, skip over the pales, turn round in a circle as well to the
right hand as to the left.

Here is an Irish Hobby.

Thou have hit the nail on the head. It Is an English Hackny. But view a little
the slouen who rides him.

He is like an Ape on a Bears back.

He starteth and stumbleth at every foot. He will show his sitter a tumbling
cast. Weehe, he kicketh: he trotteth, galloppeth, leapeth, runs, and
reareth. The poor Jade is very lean, he has nothing but bones, he is blind
of an eye: he is lame of a leg: he has all the hoofes of his feet spoiled.

He were good to run post.

True mine host. Come, along.

The Tailor. Chap. 9.
GOd speed Tailor. Welcome sir.

How many else of satin must I buy to make me a dubblet?

Four else and a quarter sir.

And how much velvet for my breeches?

If you will have them made after the Spanish fashion, you must have three
else and a half.

How much broad cloth must I have to make me a cloak after the Roman
fashion, or a riding cloak after the Dutch manner?

You must have little less then five else and a half, to make one large
enough for you with a coxcombado of the same cloth.

Well, well take measure then.

Stand upright and stir not.

How like you the Italian breech?

The Venetian guise likes me best.

What say you to the French fashion?

I will be of the English cut.

As pleases best his eyes, every one does himself disguise.

Make the codpeece of the Courtisan cut, or of the Antique manner.

Ho, let Marcantony alone.

For when shall these clothes be made?

Between this and Sunday, against Christmas, against Easter, against
Whitsontide.

Farewell then. Stay sir, I have not yet taken the measure of your purse.

There is a crown to buy lace, silk, and buttons. Steal the one half, I
pray you.

Sir I am a Tailor, I am not a thief.

You have two sciences for a need.

I have but one sir.

You are a Tailor by your trade, and a thief by your occupation. Go.

He that knows not the art, let him shut up his shop and go to cart.

The Shoemaker. Chap. 10.
WHat Cobbelero, where are you? What commands your signiory? Set a patch on my
shoo.

It shall cost you then a penny.

Shoemaker, show me some good two soled or three soled shoes, some pumps or
pantofles of Spanish leather. Let me see some boots.

Sit down on this form. Of what length is your foot?

If you will have a shoo very easy for your foot, take this same. Let me
assaie it.

Give me a shoing horn.

It will last you but too long.

There Is no so fair shoo, but comes to be a slipper.

How these? Half a crown, I will not rebate a mite.

Now tell me in good sooth, Shoemaker, did it never chance unto you after you
had so well shooed any one, as at this present you have done me, that he is
gone away without paying, or taking his leave otherwise.

No truly.

And if it should chance now, what would you do?

By God I would run after. Do you speak in good earnest. In faith I speak
it, and besides would think to do it.

Go to then, I will try once, see here is abase: follow me as quickly as you
love your shoes.

Hold the thief. Stop the thief.

Stay me not masters, I run for a wager, for a bottle of wine.

He is escaped from the Shoemaker, but he is not escaped from the thief.

Why so? He shall carry the thief with him every where, where he shall go.

It Is all one, if I can overtake him, I will lay felony to his charge.

The Debtor. Chap. 11.
WHat! ho! Sir are you gone by thus without speaking?

I speak only to those that I know.

Do you not know me?

I never saw you in my life that I can tell.

Have you forgotten that you had vvares of me lately? Of you? what is your
name? I am called Renard Wolf.

There is more then one Wolf and two Renards in the wood.

You cannot remember it? I have a weak memory.

See then your writing.

I cannot see without spectacles a whit: let me see it, soft, me thinks it is
written in Syrian.

I understand not a word.

You owe me three hundred fifty crowns: pay me, or I will cause you to be
arrested.

Of my faith you shall have them to morrow or after.

The oath is the proper arms of a liar.

I promise you if I miss now, to pay you double.

The paier double, does me vex and trouble.

Will you not trust a gentleman to be trusted, when he swears by his faith.

Faith without work is dead.

If you will not deal with me as I deal with others, do the vvorst that you
can, I care not a straw for you.

Make them that owe you pay you.

You say true, and when men have no money what shall I do to them to get money?

Cause them to be arrested, they will make shift.

Men should not be so rigorous, we ought to have compassion one of another, as
God has commanded us.

There was a good sermon made to day at our church.

O God, Christian men show themselves very Jews, far woorser than the Turks,
Moors, Arabians, Tartarians, Pagans, Sarasens, Idolaters, without any
knowledge or fear of God, who are not so cruel and barbarous in their kind as
are these Christian nations, who cut one another throat, for a devil of gold
and silver, which draws all the world after him.

You go from the text, will you pay me?

It Is a princely matter to be in debt.

He is not a good Christian who owes nothing.

The king of France owes me as good as ten thousand crowns, and the Focquers
of Germany little less.

All that is nothing to me.

I will be paid incontinent.

Tarry a little, God save me from being so incontinent out of debts.

Who lends nothing is a filthy and wicked creature, a Creature of the great
Hobgoblin of hell.

What! debts! O rare and ancient thing!

He is no good Christian who owes nothing.

Leave we these counts.

Will you pay me or not?

Give me yet three months day.

You do nothing but mock me from day to day.

Hark a word in your ear, lets talk together.

The Sergeant. Chap. 12.
I Arrest you in the Queens name, in the kings name.

Master Sergeant (for he is a gentleman by his office) I pray you do me this
favor to come with me hard by to the next streat, to the house of a Merchant
my friend, who will be suertie for me.

Dispatch then, give me half a crown from you.

Hold, there is two shillings.

Come lets go then to the Tavern to drink a quart of wine, and we will send
in the mean while for your friend.

Hark you sir, your man comes not,

My friend it is true that I can mark you prisoner.

You have taken my money for an hour.

I leese forty shillings in another place,

I have other more weighty business, what will you that I do? Give me five
shillings, and I will bring you along the City till five of the clock at
night.

Sir, I am a poor man charged with wife and children.

That's all one. Give me yet for my pain or else you shall see quickly that
which I will do.

My God what shall I do?

I am devoured alive.

What birds of pray! what crows be these?

What reason or conscience have you to take a crown more?

Gods Lord ask you that? Come, come, come along. By God sir you shall enter
into the court to see your action, I can do no less by mine office and oath.

O now you are in Cappaedocia, farewell honest man adieu, you must sing there a
little among the Canarie birds.

The Way. Chap. 13.
Fair maiden, where is the way to the well, to the church, to the river?

Right before you.

Ho pesant, countriman, countrilasse, fair woman show me the way to Rome I
pray you.

By our Lady to Rome sir, it is a great way thither. As for me I was never
there, but to go thither you must pass by many great cities and little
villages. Go your ways from hence first to Paris, and then ask the high way
to Constantinople, from thence it is five hundred good leagues and a half unto
Jerusalem. Embarke there to come for Venice or Marseilles, and you shall find
company enough every day to bring you to Rome.

It Is in mine opinion a little the farthest way about.

Which way must I go for the shortest and the directest way from hence to Rome?

Go from hence to Paris, from Paris to Lions it is but ten days iourny. From
thence to Turin in Piemont but seven, from Turin to

Florence but twelve, and from Florence to Rome but three.

The way is very hard to be kept without a guide.

Enquire of sheapheards and sheapheardesses whom you shall meet in traveling.

Is the way dry, sandy, fair, and easy to keep?

It is stony, and full of dangers by reason of thieves who lie in the vvoods.

It Is a good country that has not one mile of naughty way.

Do they do justice well in these quarters here?

As every where else.

They hang up the little thieves, but the great thieves escape well enough.

Set me in my right way I pray you.

See you the gallows there on high? Come not nere it if you be wise, pass
over the bridge, follow the pauement, hold the champaine ground, leave the
mountain, go along the vines, cross over the meddow, and you have always the
high way before you.

I doubt I shall miss my way. Men miss their way many times in going to Rome.

O God I am weary and cannot march any further.

Go back to London, and get up to the top of Paul's steeple, and from thence
take your first flight to the Tower of London, from thence fly to the castle
of Dover, from thence to the bastion of Calais in France, thence to the
Bastillia of the great city of Paris, thence to the fortress on the walls of
Orleans, thence to the citadell of Lions, thence to the great castle of
Millan, thence to the Arsenall of Venice, thence to the amphitheatre of Verona,
thence to the Theater of Pise, thence to the Castle Saint Angelo, and you shall
be quickly in Rome.

How will you that I fly, I have no vvings?

Go by Sea.

I will then embarke my self for the Straight of Gibraltar: then sing-lyng
along the coast of Catalonia, Maiorica, Mynorica, Sardinia, and Corsica, I
will land at Ciuita Vecchia, and from thence in one day, I will ride on a Mule
or an Ass to Rome.

The Ass then shall be as soon at Rome as you.

And I as soon as mine Ass.

Go then both together: I wish you a happy voyage.

The Thief. Chap. 14.
KIuala? Stand. Sblood! Swoundes!

Yield thy purse: quick, quick, dispatch, yield, alight, or I will shoot
this bullet into thy belly.

Where is thy port mantle?

You owe me three hundred fifty crowns, and shall pay me now.

Here Is nothing here? O heart I must kill this villain.

Will thou not confess where thy crowns are?

Sir, take all that I have, but spare my life.

What is that thou carriest in thy budget?

It Is my money, sir, save my life, and take it hardly, I give it you.

Thou have more sowed in thy dubblet,

Have thou not? Confess villain, I will save thy life.

Fellow give me the halter out of thy sleeue.

Cry not villain, for I will cut thy throat.

Not a word, till we be gone a great way out of sight.

He is fast bound now.

Come then fellow thief, lets mount and spur cut, lets away amain.

Whether will we go? Into France.

Let us hie us to Dover then, for if we be taken, we shall be hanged. Away.

The Mariner. Chap. 15.
HOnest men who will go for Calis, let him make have. To Calice ho.

Aboard ho.

Does the vvinde serve?

The vvinde is at Norde, North and

North West.

What take you for a passenger?

A French Crown: two Crowns man and Horse.

The ship is it well armed: for I have fear of those Pirates of Donkerke.

Fear them not, for the ship is very well equipped with Artillery and
Munition.

Go into the Prove. Lets hoyse sail on Gods name, it is calm, and blows not
a whit.

We shall have the wind by and by in Pupp: I see by the rack.

The Tide swells. See the waves mount.

The Sea begins to rise, and rage from the very bottom.

See how these huge waves beat against the sides of our ship.

Here ye these terrible whirlwindes, how they sing over our saile-yardes.

We shall have by and by a storm. The tempest makes a great noise.

The heaven begins to thunder from above.

It thundreth, it lightneth, it raineth, it haileth: it is best to strike
sail, and to vire the cables.

To the Deck ho: to the Stern: This wave will carry us to all the Devils.

O God the Savior.

O my friends: O thrice and four times happy are those who are on firm land
setting of beans?

God be merciful unto us, and our Lady of Lorretto.

Dish, dash, plash, crack, rick-rack, thwack, bounce, flounce, rounce, hizze,
pizze, vvhizze, sowze, O God help us and the Virgin Marie.

Paish, flish, flash, rowze, rittle, rattle, battle, rish, rash, clash, swish,
swash, robble, hobble, bobble. O Saint James, Saint Peter, and Saint
Christopher.

O Saint Michael, Saint Nicholas, now and never more.

O God we are now at the bottom of the Sea.

I give eighteen hundred thousand crowns of revenue to him who will set me a
land.

Lets land here: let us go a s^ore.

I will give you all that I have in the world to set me a shore.

Will you go a shore in the midst of the Ocean Sea?

What a horrible tempest?

By Saint Grison what means this?

Shall we take our sepulture here among these waves? I see neither heaven nor
earth.

I must make water a little. I pardon all the world. I die my friends.

Fare you all well.

The tempest is now ended.

O that it is fair weather again.

Truly it has lightened and thundered lustily.

I believe that all the Devils are unchained to day, or that the good Lady
Proserpina is traveling of childbirth.

Believe that all the five hundred thousand hundred millions of Devils dance
the morris.

Thunder Devils, fart, fist, fissell.

A fig for the wave. We are in the haven of Calice.

Let us more Ancres.

Cannonier, shoot of a piece of Artillery.

We are saved: I go to lodge with mine hostess at the three Kings, or at the
green Dragon.

The sick man Chap. 16.
GOd be here. Sleep you?

If I could sleep, I should be half whole.

You groan like a Hog in love with a Sow.

I do but rave, cough, spit, and sneeze.

It Is a good sign if you escape it.

I have also the squirt and the bloody flux.

What other disease have you?

I have also the itch, the great or small pockes which does fox me.

One disease comes sildome alone.

O that my head aketh cruelly.

It Is then the disease of Naples.

O who shall deliver me from my greefs?

Tarry, I have a Cemitorie which shall do the office.

Who have been your Phisicians?

The most expert of the world in vrines, for they have brought me out of an
ill week into an evil year, and have changed me a French Shanker into a
double Neapolitan Cancro.

Did you cause your self to be let blood?

The Barber has drawn three ounces of blood out of the capital vain, and
the Physician ten out of the crumenall, and has bid me take seven
Cock-chickens, etc.

What says he of your disease?

He says that it is the disease of Italy, of France, of Spain, of Germany,
and of England.

What disease is that?

The Catholic disease, the common sickness, the great malady.

Know you no other name?

They call it the pox in France, I cannot tell what they call it in England.

Speak softly, see here master Doctor.

My friend open your purse, I mean your mouth, use your legs a little.

Fair and softly, without choler, be patient, all shall be well. Take these
pills to morrow morning and you shall do well by Gods grace.

I had rather eat a baow, than to chaw and swallow down these pills, they are
so bitter.

You have wrong to find fault with good receipts and medecines, be of good
cheer, adieu.

Adieu master Doctor. O that I am sick.

Patience, alas! that I pity you pretty fellow.

There is no remedy now.

Couragio, couragio, you shall be well quickly.

Sickness comes always on horseback, and goes away on foot.

The Draper. Chap. 4.
GEntleman, sir, signior, honest man, what cloth will you see? want you any
good sorts of clothes?

Come hither, I will use you well.

Of what color will you have?

Show me a fair scarlet, a welsh frise, a good Irish rug.

Have you a fair French tawnie? Let me see a very fine London green. Show me
a Crimsin very fine and large.

I have the best in London for you.

There is one of a good breadth, look upon it well in the light. Handle it,
the color is in grain.

It will not loose color.

How sell you the yard, the elle, the whole piece, the half yard, a
gowne-cloth, a cloake-cloth?

At a word I would sell it, ten shillings six pence the yard, fifteen
shillings the elle, sixteen shillings eight pence the elle and the half
quarter.

Well measure out five else and an half, make good measure I pray you.

One, two, three, four, five, and a half, good measure.

What would you have mistress, a stammell to make you a petticote, or a purple
for a kertle? Of what color will you have, of white, black, gray, violet,
green, of medlie color, of rats or ships color, yellow, blew, orange,
purple, crimsin, sky color, lions hair, etc. I have of all colors, and of
all prises.

How gracious and glib are the tongues of these young drapers in Watling-street?

The Inn. Chap. 17.
GOd save you mine host. Welcome sir.

Shall I be lodged here for this night?

How many are you?

Harrie, my beast, Ronsin and I.

You shall be used well. Come in if you please, they shall draw off your boots
and spurs by and by.

Hostler, I pray thee rub my horse well and give him a bottle of hay and a
pecke of oats.

I will look to him well sir, doubt not of it.

Mine host when shall we sup? I have a good stomach.

When it shall please you sir. The supper is ready. Well let us wash our hands.

Come come to table masters.

Shall not we stay for the other?

Yes we will, as the Abbot stays for his monks, to wit, in eating as fast as
he can.

Gentlemen eat if you please, drink round, you are not merry.

Make good cheer of that which you have, it is fasting day to day, it is our
Ladies even, There are eggs in the shell, butterd, poched, and fride. I must
eat some flesh, for I love not eggs nor fish.

There is then a very good caponet.

It Is a very dainty meat.

True, when the stomach is in good disposition.

I am of opinion that a roasted capon, is better to be eaten then a raw leg of
mutton.

And I had rather drink wine then water.

There is a fat capon, a Turkie-cocke, a fat goose, a dosen of larkes, a couple
of good fat rabbets.

Begin where you will.

And here is a calves head.

What is a good head with a good brain worth?

It is inestimable: but the head without the tongue, never made oration long.

Who will eat any good fish? There is a good Lamprey. It is the pray of a king,
and here is a good side of Salmon, which is worth the having of Solomon.

Flesh makes flesh and fish makes fish, and good wine makes good wine and
vinagre.

There is Frogs and snailes.

They eat them in France and in Italy.

But in England they care not for them.

Ho Friar John eat you not of this good fish?

To tell you the reason, there is such a season, that fish is poison.

Taste of this trowt, sup of this Eele-broath, it will heat your guts, and wash
them sweetly, to bring your belly in temper.

Of all birds I love not the goose nor the gosling.

Of all fishes I esteem the Eele poison. Of all fresh fish except the tench,
take the wing of a Partridge, or the buttocke of a Nun. I love woonderfully
the white of the capon. Therein are you nothing like Foxes, for of capons,
hens and chickens that they take, they never eat the white.

Why so? because they have no cooks to dress them.

Boy some wine, fill to me. To you mine host To you mine hostess. I will
pledge you here. I believe it freeseth, it is so cold, let us rise masters,
and draw near to the fire.

The God of Paradise that's best,
Be for all these graces blessed.
The going to bed. Chap. 18.
I Feel my self a little ill after supper.

My stomach greeueth me.

Mine Oast I will go to rest. I have great desire to sleep. The sleep is
already fallen into mine eyes.

Let some body show me my chamber.

When you will my daughter shall show you the way.

God night mine Ost. God night, God-night hostess.

God give you good night and good rest every body.

You must mount this way sir. See your chamber.

See your bed. There are the priuies, and here is your chamber pot.

Draw these curtines. Lend me a kercheffe or a coiffe: I have a night cap in my
bosom.

Your sheets are clean.

Look that they be very dry I pray you.

I have ayred them at the fire.

Pull of my hosen. Cover me with my gown.

I have too little covering.

Give me another pillow, I cannot lie so low.

Are you well now? will you have yet more heling? would you nothing else?

Not now. Seepe well.

Hark Gaudinetta, kiss me once my sweet heart, before thou depart. I had
rather die, then kiss a man in his bed.

Kiss me, and I will give you your bracelets again, that I took from you the
other day playing with you.

Speak no more to me, I pray you of kissing nor of love, but give me my
bracelets again, for otherwise what will my father and my mother say to me?

They will be angry with me.

They will not: they will not.

But they will I assure you: but it is no matter, I will say that a thief has
stolen them from me.

Hark, I pray thee, Gaudinetta, tell me one thing, which of us two loves one
another better: whether you me, or I you?

As for me I do not hate you: for as Gods commandes, I love all the world.

But to the purpose, are you not inamoured of me?

I have told you so many times, that you should not use any such speeches to
me, if you speak any more to me, I shall show you that it is not to me, that
you should address your self.

You are very obstinate, I see well.

Give me my bracelets again then if you will.

How now my love your bracelets? I will not, I swear a great oath: but I
will give you others.

Had you not rather have a silken girdle, etc.

Hark some body calls me.

They do not.

Gaudinetta what do you above so long?

I come by and by mother.

Will you not kiss me before you depart.

Another time. I am called now. I shall be chidden. I cannot kiss men.

My father will be angry. My mother comes.

What will you do? Let me go. I shall be killed by and by.

God give you good night, Sir. God night fair maiden. God night, Gaudinetta.

The Slasher. Chap. 19.
GOd morrow to your Seignorie, Seignior Vespasian.

In faith friends meet oftener then mountains.

By the faith of a Gentleman I am very glad to see your magnificence lusty.

Let me give you an accollado. What Signior Valerian my dear friend, my near
cousin. Come, cullion let me crush thy callibisters with accoling thy
buttockes. Shake hands.

Love passes the glove, and the water goes, clean through the bootehose.

You make no more reckoning of petty compagnions.

No, no, I live with but with Princes and among great Lords.

Whence comes your signorie?

I come from seeing the seventeen lands of the low countries.

You are grown too rich.

A fig for money, I shall have one day but too much: for I have a Philosophers
stone which fetches money to me out of men's purses, as fast as the Adamant does
draw iron unto it.

You are sick as far as I see by your phisiognomie, and I know your disease.

What I pray thee? you have a flux of the purse, as well as I.

Care not you for that, I have yet six pence half pen^e, which never saw
father nor mother, which shall want you no more then the Griegoes in your
necessity.

Thy male Mules, poultron. I have more money then thou vvottest of: for I have
three score and three ways to recover money always at my need.

The most honorable and common is, in robbing by the high way side.

The Canker claw thee villain: Tell, have thou no money poor Devil?

What then, Fortune will favor me one day to have a purse as long as mine
arm, full of fair Angels or French Crowns.

You have spoken mask. What will thou say to a fellow who will show thee ten
or twelve budgets full of old Rose nobles, Chikinoes, and Hungary Ducates?

If thou show them me, I will pick out mine eyes.

See, look here villain. Bless thee, make the sign of the cross on thy
nose.

I think thou have more callibisters then Crowns.

For those are counters or palace pence that thou have there. My friend hark,
thou have no pastime at all in this world. I have more then the King.

And if thou would consort with me, we would play the Devils.

No, no, by Saint Adauras, for thou shall be hanged once.

He that's borne to be hanged, shall never be drownde. Ah great sheep!

Fear thou hanging? Thou shall be buried, which is more honorable, the air
or the earth?

I had rather perish in the water among the golden sands of the river of
Plata, in the West Indies.

Will thou come along into Flanders with me?

What to do?

To beat the pauement: we will make the dust fly.

Seignior good face will thou change me a crown?

Believe me I have no small money.

I would have testons or kardekews.

I will not change you a halfepen^e nor a farthing by God.

Lend me then six pence, I pray you.

I have no silver about me believe me.

Yet you must needs for all that lend me sixpence to pay mine ordinary whether
you will or not.

By Gogs nownes I have it not.

Go you are no better then a rogue.

Go thou art a villain thy self.

Will you say that I am a villain?

He is a villain which does any villainy, or who has no money to spend with
his friends, and if thou be noble show thy gentilitie, pay but only a pint.

I am as blind as a beetle I tell thee.

Of what live thou now?

Of the air as does the Sturgion.

If I did not fear to lie against mine honor, I would say that thou were an
honest man.

If you should say any otherwise, I would make you lie in your throat.

Give not me the lie I pray you companion.

Take not in evil part that which I have said.

By the death of my life give not me the lie, for I will stob thee into the
throat.

Say you so, here is a fair place.

Draw, I will break thy head in nine places.

Cap de saint Arnauld, you shall pass through my hands now. Come rogue, come,
push, push.

Ho saint Siobe cap de Gascoigne, come good fellow devil defend your self.

By the flesh I renounce, by the blood I deny, by the body I refuse my part of
Papimanie, if you escape my hands now, I will stick thee like a hog.

See that my rapier be no longer then thy sword, I hate worse then death him
that fights upon the advantage of knives.

Come, come, push, let us fight gallant, and lustily rub our bacon.

I will not fight, let us rather drink on our swords.

I care not, if thou vvilt fight, lets fight, if thou vvilt drink lets drink.

We are well met together, one blind man leads another.

It Is danger least we fall both into some Tavern.

They would give us drink then to be rid of us.

Have thou credit no where?

I have as much as the King, but it is far hence.

I think it is beyond the pillars of Hercules, or in the kingdom of Persia.

On this side a little, in Polonia or in Spruceland.

I, I, in Cracouia.

It is thither of mine oath more then five hundred leagues of France.

Lets go, were it as far as the East Indies.

Go you then alone, for I will not bear you company.

See here a drole, he will pay always pint, shopin, or quart, we shall
dominier by and by.

God save you sir, I met the other day your capital ennemie, and gave him the
fig under his cappuche bravely, I gave him also a stoccado through his right
arm, which ran clean through his doublet into his left elbow, and had almost
slain him outright. I did it by God in your quarrel, and for the love of you,
for otherwise understand, I had not hazarded my self for an hundred crowns.

Lets go drink, lets go drink at the Sun on this side Criplegate.

Mistres let some body draw us a shopine.

Sir I drink to you.

Seignior to your good grace. Thanks my good captain, I will pledge you here.

Ha monsieur my friend, you know well that I have always loved you, and taken
you for a very honest man.

We are friends by God, and will be as long as we live in despite of the
great devil of hell.

I swear unto you by Styx and Acheron in your presence, that there is a blade,
to the purpose, if you stand in need, it is at your gentle commandment, my
carkase and goods, tripes and bowels, and all.

You have truly a very fine sword.

Look well upon that same tool, look on it well, I swear unto you that it
was the very tucke of the prince of Parma, and from his own proper side he
gave it me into mine own proper hands.

For some piece of service that you had done him peradventure.

Well does it please you to command me any thing that I may do for you? for I
am going sir.

Your seigniorie will be content to do me this favor to lend me an hundred
crowns till my return from Venice.

By my beard, if I had them you should have them willingly.

Your seigniory lend me then a horse.

I have, believe me, neither mu^e nor mare, neither man, boy, nor beast.

O patience of pennilesse mates! then adieu till we meet again.

I pray the god Mercury, with Dis the father of crowns, in saunty long time
to conserve your seigniorie.

The Bragger. Chap. 20.
SAint George, come, some body bring me my lance, my two-hand sword, and my
cuirasse, I will be armed from top to toe.

Truly here is fair work cut out for martial men, now we should march
against that Thracian dog, Mahound God of Turks and of Arabians, we are called
away into France to aid the French king against those Saracine leaguers. Oh
would to God that Carolus quintus were alive.

O what a marciall head! O what a mad Rowland! I should be sufficiently
instructed in military affairs, having commanded twenty five years
captain general among the Hungarians and Poles, and hasarded my proper person
in more then thirty bloody battles.

Come, come, mine arms of malice, I say of maile.

The brave soldior ought to be armed no less with advise then with armor.

Where I cannot prevail with the skin of the Lion, I will use the case of
Renard the Fox.

Consider vvisely the chaunses of hasard: one must never prosecute them to
their period: for it behooves all Caualiers, reverently to entreat their good
fortune, without racking or tormenting it at all.

I take advise always before I march, of that which is to be done, to the end
that I be not like to the Athenians, who did never go into counsel til^ after
the deed was past.

I am wise. You are so.

I am full of chivalry and a spruce soldier. You are so.

I am courageous, virtuous, and magnanimious.

You are so.

I am resolute, noble, generous and magnificent.

You are so.

I am loyal, honorable, well armed, high mounted, well disposed, hasardous,
audacious, heroical, crafty, and cawtelous as the Serpent which tempted Eve.

You are so.

I am come of a noble race: For mark me well: There was a certain Chalbrot
who engendered Sarabrot, the father in law of Nymbrot, not of him who built the
Tower of Babell: but of another of the race of Gallafre, the cousin of
Brulefer, who was brother to Maschefain, grandsire of Sortibrand of Conymbria,
the Nephew of Atlas, who with his shoulders held up the heaven from falling.
The same A^las being bred and borne in Marocco in Barbary. (You Gentlemen knew
him well) was cousin german to Gogmagog, who with his two hands set, it is long
since, the two hills of chalk near Cambridge a most famous University in
England, to the end that the scholars should walk thither some times to pass
their times about them.

The same was gossip to Fierabras, of whom Merlinus Coccaius writes in his
book of the country of devils, that he was the first in this world that played
at dice with spectacles on his nose, of whom descended afterward the great
Giant Oromedon, father of Briareus, who had a hundred hands, and Offot the god
son of Coryneus, of whom you may see the image in the Yeeld-hall of London:
who is my kinsman a far of: for of his noble lineage am I descended.

I have read of your ancestors in the Iestes of the four sons of Aymon.

Find you not that they have been very famous for their horrible feats of
Arms?

They have showed their singular prowess truly against the Sarasins.

I rage, Devils I rage, hold me Devils, hold me.

Ho Caetzo great Devil of hell, awake thy sleepie Cyclopes: Thou Vulcan who
limpest with thy cosins Asteropes, Brontes, Steropes, Polyphemus and Pyracmon.
I will set you a work. I give my self to an hundred pipes of old Devils, in
case that if you will not fight, if I do not make you eat the two eggs of
Proserpina.

Truly Hercules is nothing to you, who being in the cradle, killed the two
Serpents: for the said Serpents were very little and vveake things.

Where is this so furious Hercules? I would fight with him for a little
quarter of an hour.

He would make you piss vinegre before all the world.

Where is Hector that Trojan Lad? I have a great desire to break a Lance
against his Cuirace.

Where is Alexander, the great drunka^d of Greece?

I will make him drink a carouse. To marciall men we must not spare good
wine.

Where is Achilles the Grig, Captain of the Mirmidons, I would send his soul
by and by into hell.

Where is this petty companion Ulysses? He should do me a message unto Pluto.

Where is this quaking-quiuering coward Julius Caesar? that I might horse him
on the end of my Pike and Lance.

You count without your host.

I am afraid that before it be night, you will be firked with Musket and
Harquebus shot.

Ho! that I am not a king of France for four score and ten or a hundred
years: by God I would make curtald curres of you Gentlem^n that ran away
from Pauy.

I hate him more then poison, who runs away when it is come to slashing with
knives.

I fear death no more then a butterfly, or the tickling of a flea in mine
ear: and as for me, I fear not to fight with a whole Army, if it be not of
these mescreant Tartarians, Canniballes, Indians, and Moscouites who shoot
forward, backward, sideling, this way, that way, every way, at long, at
large, over, under.

Of a troth the prouesse of Camillus, Scipio, Pompey* Caesar, and Themistocles,
are nothing comparable to yours.

I kill men, as Diomedes killed the Thracians, and as Vlyxes, I tie their
carcasses to my horse heels, as Homere recounteth.

I vvish you for all that, if the enemies yield themselves, that you take them
to mercy and ransom, for clemency is a very royal virtue.

Ho death of a louse, blood of a bat, by the virtue, my friend if I put not all
to fire and sword, I renounce my life.

You affright me with swearing so much.

Thou art a beast of the mountain.

These are but heroical words, and colors of martial Rhetoric to adorn
my language.

To put them all to the edge of the sword, it is too cruel a thing that.

I am a martial man, what would you have? Sassassa, how many are these
quistrell-scummes?

By the death of a Calfe, I esteem them less then a roasted Capon, for They
are but bragging fools of France,
Hardy at the bottle, and cowards at the Lance.


My friend, I shock so rudely upon the enemies, that I overthrow them like
hogs: to some by and by I spatter their brain, to others I break their arms,
to others I cut off their noses, pick out their eyes, cleave their jaws,
strike their teeth into their head, and if any will save himself in running
away, I make his head fly in five hundred four score and nine pieces.

If any one cry Saint George, I set my foot upon his gorge: If he cry Saint
Barbara, I pull off his beard: If our Lady of Loretto, I cut off his head.

Make you ready, the enemies are at hand.

I am armed at advantage, the Lance in rest, mounted as a S. George.

See the Camp of the enemies: they are encamped on a molehill. O it is but a
microcosme of flies or vvaspes!

They are not in so great number, as had Xerxes, are they?

He had, if you credit Herodotus and Trogus Pompeius, thirty hundred thousand
fighting men, and nevertheless Themistocles with a handful of men discomfited
them.

It is enough, I will bring you them to roast or to boil, to fry or to put in
paste. But hear the Drum: the enemies Camp is not far hence.

The battles are ranged. The martial combat begins pel-mell.

The Artillery begins to play against the walls.

There is a Cannon shot which has carried away quite the vangard to the Devil.

The alarm begins to be very hot. The Trumpets sound the retraict.

The Infanterie is almost all overthrown.

The cornets of light horse retire.

They fetch their carrier toward me. O what rain of lead! what a smoke of
lightening and fire! By God I will run away. Demosthenes says that the man
that runs away will fight again. I am afeard to be slain and murdered.

What runaway is this? what coward is this?

Courage, courage, why tremble you?

I am affeard to die.

Sassassa, kill, kill.

O masters save my life. I have the word.

I am of your side, a friend.

Who are you? whence are you?

what do you here?

I am the seignior Crocodill.

Ha what say thou?

Masters I am a poor devil, I beseech you have pity on me, I have yet a
crown to pay my bienvenu.

You are a spy, I will cut your throat.

O monsieur my friend, I yield me to you.

Thou shall be feign to yield villain, for thou shall also yield thy soul to
all the devils.

Alas, I am cruelly vvounded.

I am dead, slain, massacred. I go to take the way of the first hospital.

The Conclusion of the Parliament of Pratlers.
Come hither ho, let us sit down a little in the shade under this tree, in the
cool air. I will sit upon this block.

And I over against thee Antonie.

Where will thou sit Peter?

I? I will set my self upon this stone.

Sit away from thence, you let me that I cannot behold the passers by.

I see a ship which sayleth toward us.

It Is a Caracke of two thousand tuns.

See you the little boy with a flagge in his hand.

If he fall into the sea he will be drowned.

No I warrant you, for he can swim.

O how he slides down the tackling.

The mast of this ship is very great and thick.

I think it grew at the West Indies.

How can thou tell, have thou been in India with the Spaniards?

As if one can know nothing of a country, if he have not been there. I
discern Powles from hence.

There are me thinks some pigeons upon the top of the steeple.

They be men, fond and foolish boy, but they seem to them that see them so far
off to be crows, or rather lacke-dawes.

Hark some body sounds a trumpet on the steeple, what an ass is that?

He sounds a point of war for the mistresses of London.

O what fine weather here is! it is pleasant being among these green fields.

Give ear to that Lark which sings. O how she mounteth on high up to the
Clouds! I remember now Bartasius the French Poet, who wrote these four most
excellent verses of her note, The pretty Lark man's angry mood does charm
with melody,
Her Tee-ree-lee-ree Tee-ree-lee-ree chippring in the sky,
Up to the court of love sweet bird, mou^ting with flickering wings,
And down again, my Jove adieu, sweet Jove adieu, she sings,


O how this little pretty chippering Lark quauereth her voice.

Where art thou Linus most harmonious musition with thine yuorie lute? where
art thou Amphio^ the finest* fingrer of an harp in all Greece? where art thou
Orpheus with thy silver sitterne? where art thou Arion with thy melody that
made the fishes danse in the sea? come hither, learn new lessons. Truly these
four who have been so famous by report of all Latin and Greek authors, for
that they were most excellent musitions, have never been able to counterfeit
this little bird singing her note.

Hark, hark, it is some other bird that sings now.

It Is a blacke-bird or a Nightingale.

The Nightingale sings not but evening and morning.

Where is she I pray thee?

It Is a Nightingale I heard her record.

See thou not her sitting on a sprig?

O how sweetly she sings without any stop, and cease not!

We must not marvel though she sing sweetly, for that she is of Athens, where
the waves of the sea flote also against the shores by measure.

Pliny vvriteth that she sings before men longer, and more sweetly a great
deal.

What is the reason thereof? I will tell it thee.

The Cookow and the Nightingale sing at one season of the year, to wit, in
the spring time, from the midst of April to the end of May, or thereabout.
These two birds contended about the sweetness of their song, they seek a
judge, and because their dispute was of notes, there was found fit to judge
this controversy the Ass, who has above all other beasts his ears long.
The Ass having put back the Nightingale, whose music he said he did not
understand, judged the victory to the Cookow. The nightingale appealed before
man, whom when she saw, she pleads her cause and sings sweetly, to make
her cause good, and to be revenged of the vvrong that the Ass had offered her.

I hear now a thing worthy of a poets vain.

What then? did thou look for something smelling of a philosophers brain?
Seek that of these new magistri inertes of the university.

Many of them are philosophers in their gowns, and by their wits country
clownes.

Say then that they are doctors of Valentia, with long gowns and little
scientia.

But harken me yet our Nightingale.

I will tell thee something of the song of the Nightingale, translated out of a
good author.

What man in the world (says he) is so gros headed, so dull spirited, so
blockish or doltish, that is not astonished and ravished with an unspeakable
delight, hearing the melody of the nightingale, and how such a shrill and
harmonious voice may issue out of so small a trunk? beside that he perseuereth
so earnestly in his song, that his life shall so soon fail him as his voice:
so that it seems that he has been taught by some master musition to sing
his prick-song: for one while he faineth the base, another while he quauereth
the contretenour, by and by he counterfeiteth the mean, anon he sings the
treble. And being weary with recording, changes quickly his note, that you
would guess him to be some other bird that sings but the plain song. Then
suddenly he chaunteth such shrill notes, that he astonieth himself, and
remains as it were in a sweet trance, charmed by an infinity of his
melodious passages, which ravish the spirits up to the heavens, not only of
men, but also of other little fine birds, whom he inchanteth and takes
prisoners there by his voice, and makes them through his sweet note to harken
to him, and to steal away a lesson or two of his music. And not content with
this, you shall see her instruct her young ones, entising them to imitate her
note, to keep tune with her, to make the same stops, to quauer in length, one
while to run upon crochets, by and by to take a new tune by fainings, then to
descant and semiquauer it with courbed minims, quickly trembles her voice and
transformeth it into so many sorts of musical points, that no artifice of man
can tell how to counterfeit her note.

I am almost ravished truly.

Without doubt men have learned Music of Birds.

Democritus was the Nightingales scholar, witness Aristophanes in his
Comedy of the song of Birds. I have caught a Grasse-hopper in one hand, and a
little Frog in the other.

Let me see them I pray thee!

I will put them into thy bosom, Anthony.

Do not, I pray thee.

Why runnest thou away? Come hither, I will do thee no harm.

Alas! the poor prisoners! let them go: let them leap.

Look! what a fine skip there is?

The poor beasts have saved themselves in the grass.

This bank is full of Pisse-mires.

They sting me by the buttockes.

They got in at my codpeece. I have more then a thousand in my breeches.

Do you feel them sting in good faith?

I have pulled off the heads of more then twenty.

Kill them not, let them live. It Is a pretti^ kind of vermin.

You have troubled the Lords of their Parliament, which are assembled in this
hillocke for the affairs of their common wealth. They are angry with you.

They will tickle you indeed.

Let us go to some other place.

Let us sit in the Sun under this wall.

Sit away brother Nathanael. Why?

See you not the great Spider that hangs by a little Spider-webbe?

O the great thief! He is a brave hunter for Flies and Butter-flies.

Have you never seen the combat fought between the Spider and the buzzing Fly?

No: then you have never seen the battle fought between Caesar and Pompey.

Why are they such brave vvarriours?

Far greater then the Rat and the Frog in Aesops Fables.

Behold this cruel Tyrant! How many Bees, Flies and Butterflies he has killed?

See their carcasses lie dead in his nets.

It Is a strange thing of the nature of this Animal.

Come tell us something of him my fine prattling slave.

It Is a thing almost monstrous in nature, of the nature of these Spiders, unto
whom women and maidens are scholars, and have learned of them to spin, and to
weaue linen cloth, and the fishers also to make their nets: but they have a
far greater grace and expedition in their industries: The spinner the spiders
wife, and her daughter the young spidreffe they spin and make thrids and cobwebs
for the toil and the snare, whilst her husband hunteth abroad some where for
his and their living: and is lurking secretly in some little hole in the wall,
to vvatch and to catch some beast to en^rap him in his net. And although his
body be little bigger then a peaze, yet has he for all that such spirit and
liveliness that sometime he takes great buzzing flies and little lezards in
his nets, and marketh so well his time to chase, that he seems to be an
Astrologian: he is contrary to us that tarry for fair weather, for he chaceth
when it is cloudie and darkesome, which presageth unto us rain, according to
the opinions of the two great Philosophers Aristotle and Pliny.

Hear thou the Frogs croke?

It Is sign of fair vveather or of rain, or of vvind, or of storm, or of
nothing.

See you that dog there, who pisseth against the wall?

He is as Plato says, in his second book of his Commonwealth, the most
Philosophicallest beast in the world.

But did you never see a dog meeting with a mary-bone, if you have seen him,
you might note with what devotion he does vvatch it: with what care he keeps
it: with what zeal he holds it: with what policy he sets his seete on
it: with what wisdom he tameth it: with what affection he bruseth it, and
with what diligence he sucketh it.

What moves him to do so? what is the hope of his study? what good pretends
he?

Nothing more then a little marrow. True it is, that this little is more
delicious then a great deal of all other things.

The reason why: because that the marrow is a meat labored to the perfection
of nature.

As says Galenus 3. lib. fac. etc.

Of all beasts there is none more sage, nor more subtle then the lack-anapes.

O the vilanous beast! He does nothing but mock every body: and shows his
tail to every one that goes by: he keeps not the house as the dog: he
draws not the plough as the Ox: he yields neither milk nor wool as the
sheep: he carries not his master as the Horse: he bears no burden as the
Ass. That which he does is but to defile and mar all: which is the cause
why he receives of every one so many blows and mowes.

Can you tell what my fellow-companion Maximilian told me?

What I pray thee.

That Ieronimo Pierruche is miserably inamoured of a pretty wench, and that
his brother Jack, he that played at nineholes with us, is bound with a
merchant, having quite left his learning: that is, he is promoted from the hall
into the kitchen.

What is this I hear?

This Ieronimo you knew him well fat slave, cherrie cheeked, fair and well
liking, merry, with a slicke face, pleasant, disposed, and a tratling
companion: Now he is lea^e, won, pale, looking like one half dead, vveake,
ugly, dreaming, loving to be alone, and cares for no bodies company: so that
none of those that had seen him before, could now know him again.

O the poor and wretched young man! Of what proceeds his grief?

Of love. Of love? Tell troth.

Now he is mad: he is foolish: oftentimes he walks alone: but will never
speak to any body: always mumbling or recording some thing in English verse,
that he has made to his sweete-heart and minion.

O caitiffe boy!

One while you shall see him feign a sea of tears, a lake of miseries, wring
his hands and vveep, accuse the heaven, curse the earth, make an anatomy of
his heart, to freeze, to burn, to adore, to play the Idolater, to admire, to
feign heavens, to forge hels, to counterfeit Sisyphus, to play the Tantalus, to
represent Titius Tragedy.

And by and by he exalts in his verses that Diana whom he loves best: her
hair is nothing but goldwire, her brows arches and vautes of Ebenus: her eyes
twinkling stars like Castor and Pollux, her looks lightnings: her mouth
Coral: her neck Orient-Pearle: her breath Baulme, Amber, and Musk: her
throat of snow: her neck milke-white: her dugs that she has on her breast,
Mountains or Apples of Alablaster. All the rest of her body is but a
prodigality and treasure of heaven and of nature, that she had reserved to
work the perfection of his mistres and dear.

It Is great danger least he fall beside himself in the end.

O the poor passionate is cruelly eclipsed! One while you shall see him drowned
in tears and lamentations, to make the air echo with his sighs, complaints,
murmurings, rages, imprecations: otherwhiles if he have got but a glaunce of
his goddess, you shall see him gay, glistering like an Emerawd, and pleasant,
sometime you shall see him cross, pass and repasse five or six times a day
through a street that he may have but one friendly look of her eye that he
loves best.

What will you give me if I show you a letter that he wrote to his sweet-hart.

I pray thee my minion do me this favor that I may see it.

I will read it out aloud, hearken.

Mistress your beauty is so excellent, so singular, so celestial, that I
believe Nature has bestowed it on you as a sampler to show how much she can do
when she will employ her full power and best skill. All that is in your self
is but honey, is but sugar, is but heavenly ambrosia. It was to you to whom
Paris should have judged the golden apple, not to Venus, no, nor to Juno, nor
to Minerua, for never was there so great magnificence in Juno, so great wisdom
in Minerua, so great beauty in Venus, as in you. O heavens, gods and
goddesses, happy shall he be to whom you grant the favor to col you, to kiss
you, and to lie with you. I cannot tell whether I am predestined by the
Fairies, wherefore I commend me to your good grace, and kissing your white
hands, humbly I take my leave without Adieu.

He understands already the courtisane Rhethoricke, the poor boy is blind,
and out of his best wit.

He will call himself home one day.

I shall be very glad for his sake truly.

I retire into the City, for we have been too long in this place.

Let us to go to Powles to see the Antiquities.

Let us go up into the Quire.

Who is buried within this wall?

It is Seba king of Saxons, who conquered this country of England.

See what a goodly toombe there is truly. Who is entombed here?

John of Gant duke of Lancaster, and son to king Henry the third.

See there his lance and his target of horn.

What Epitaph is this?

Of sir Philip Sidney, the peerless paragon of letters and arms.

Let us read it I pray you: England, Netherland, the Heavens, and the Arts,
The Soldiers, and the World, have made six parts
Of the noble Sydney: for none will suppose,
That a small heap of stones can Sydney enclose.
His body has England, for she it bred,
Netherland his blood, in her defense shed:
The Heavens have his soul, the Arts have his fame,
All Soldiers the grief, the World his good name.


It Is great pity of this young gentleman's death.

He is dead, and it is too late to call him from the dead.

Whose new arms be these?

Of my lord Chancellor of late memory, a man who deserved much more then I
can speak of at this time.

What black fellow is this?

It Is master John Collet, who built the school in Paules-churchyard.

Truly this Church is very long.

It is longer by five paces then our Lady Church at Paris.

How can you tell?

I have measured them both, but this is not so large nor built so neatly and
finely.

Yet did Englishmen build them both.

Let us go walk below in the body of the Church.

What ancient monument is this?

It is, as some say, of duke Humfrie of Gloucester who is buried here.

They say that he has commonly his Lieftenant here in Paul's, to know if there
be any news from France or other strange countries.

It Is true my friend, and also he has his Steward who inuiteth the bringers of
these news to take the pains to dine with his grace.

Keeps he a magnificent house?

Open house from five a clock in the morning till six a clock at night.

But for strangers I mean.

For strangers as well as for those of his own household and for citizens.

See me there three companions who are asleep in that corner upon a block. I
cannot tell whether they be footmen or travelers without horses.

They are of the dukes train.

They be knights of the post.

You are a skoffing companion, in matters of green apes faces.

Hark, if you stand in need of bale or surety: for five shillings these
fellows will lend you a false oath to bale you if it were ten thousand crowns.

You are a very Jew go.

And you are an eater of Serpents.

Know you that fellow who walks there clothed in satin? He has eaten his
corn in the^ grass.

He has made then fine greene-sauce. See how he squareth it like an
Historiographer. He is an Alchymist by his mine, and has multiplied all to
mooneshine.

He has then blown fair, and lives now as fish by the air.

I monder much at one thing, and it is, that there are so many false coyners of
money in England, and so few Alchymists.

Alchymists, I trust, are not coyners.

No, but sophisticators of metals are false lads.

I Will stand to it, that Chimistres are honest men.

I say neither good nor bad of them, let them multiply till their hearts ache,
and their purses crake.

Have you never studied Alchymistrie?

No, for it is a pure vanity.

O the sweet science! O the rich deceit to make the folosophers stone!

How might one do to make it? I will say to find it out.

You must bestow much blowing on all kind of metals.

Let me alone then, is it all in blowing well?

To tell you troth, this secret has been discovered in old time, and in our
days also, but by two or three only: Who were God-a-mighties godsonnes.

How call you this secret?

It Is called humane blood, the water of life, the dragon, the crow, the Elixir,
the mercury of the Philosophers, the drie-water, the multiplying spirit, the
tree of life, the living water, the seed of gold, the holy remedy of all
diseases.

Tell me, I pray you, what are the virtues of this stone?

This stone is of such virtue, that if men mix one part among a thousand
parts, yea ten thousand parts of quicksilver or of Tin, of lead, of iron,
of copper well prepared, they shall be incontinent and in a moment turned into
pure gold, fine, and of better alloy then any natural, or myner all gold can
be.

Has it no other properties?

A thousand: for in less then a minute it heal^th all diseases, preuenteth
those that would come: prolongeth the life: and in such wise, that whosoever
shall use of this gold, shall live many hundred years, as did the holy
Patriarchs.

Have you ever known any that had that receipt?

The Emperor keeps a man prisoner at Prage in Bohemia, who could make.

What? Gold: the Philosophers Stone: the Elixir, the powder of Proiection: the
great work and secret of the Chymisters.

Why is he prisoner then?

Because he has made gold purer by Art, then it was by Nature.

It Is great pity truly. But let us prate a little more before we go any
further.

Content.

See you that man there? He is neither too little, nor too big.

No: for he is of an indifferent stature.

He is a fine man of his person, and has none other fault but that he is a
thief, a drunkard, a cogger, a dicer, a cutter, if there were ever any in
London.

He has his fingers made for the nonst as Minerua or Araohne, and bath
heretofore cried Triacle, and in sum, is the finest maister-flie in England,
and carries always a picklocke in his pocket, wherewith he will pick open
any door. Let him not come near your fathers Coffers if you be wise.

He has threescore and three ways to find out money always, but he has two
hundred and fourteen ways to spend it.

Now let us return to our lodgings: we will go through Cheap Side and by the
Bourse.

We shall see as we go some fair faces.

Fie villain, that shall be dishonor to me who am a young Academike.

See where a fine girl passes by: she has a smug and a glistering face.

I will not look upon her: for in my mind it is a most filthy thing for young
Students to stand gazing after foolish and fond girls.

See thou this Cross?

It Is one of the fairest monuments of the City of London.

It is the Ornement of the great, large, and the fairest street of the City.
It Is all gilden without side.

I never saw the like.

It cost more then ten thousand crowns the building and trimming.

It Is much that, I think thou lie now.

Did thou never see the Pyramides which is in Saint Peter's Churchyard at
Rome?

No: for I was never there.

I have been there: and I have seen it too: and I say that the Cross in
Cheap brings more beauty and ornament to the City of London, then does
Julius Caesar's Pyramides to Rome.

See the Standard of Cheap Side hard by.

I see it well: it is a Condit of water.

They say it has been surety and pledge for many good fellows, when they
have had small store of money in their purses, and few friends to put in any
pledge and bale for themselves.

Now we are got to the Change.

The Exchange was built in the year 1567. by the Knight sir Thomas Gressam;
who has also given large revenues to the City of London, for the
maintenance of a College and Schoole-maisters to read therein the seven
liberal Sciences.

London shall then be an University.

That's true: but God he knows when. Look the Merchants vviues which walk
here. They are very pleasant, and beautiful truly. Know you not the
Pilgrimesse who passes there?

She is young, frisking, elegant, gallant, too too gracious toward her
neighbors: you never saw a more proper woman, a finer minion, more quick at
her hand and her needle then this is.

See a fine counterfeit of a Cuckold who embraces her!

Truly one may hereafter take Lions by their crags, Horses by their maines:
Bears by their nosthrils: Buffes by their mussels, Wolves by their tails:
Goats by their beardes:

Birds by their feet: Asses by their ears: men by their words.

And where will you take hold of Oxen and Cuckolds then?

By the horns.

It Is pratled, chatted, and babbled enough. Lets go this way: lets go that
way: Along, along.

The praise of France, translated out of the works of William Salustius, Lord
of Bartas.
O Fruitful France! Most happy Land, happy and happy thrice!
O pearl of rich European bounds! O earthly Paradise!
All hail sweet soil! O France the mother of many conquering knights,
Who planted once their glorious standards like triumphing vvights,
Upon the banckes of Euphrates where Titan Day-torch bright
Rises, and bloody swords vnsheathd where Phoebus drounds his light.
The mother of many Artist-hands whose workmanship most rare
Dimmes Natures works, and with her fairest flowers does compare.
The Nurse of many learned wits who fetch their skill divine
From Rome, from Greece, from Egypt far, and ore the learned shine,
As does the glymmering-Crimsin-dye over the darkest gray:
Titan ore stars, or Phoebus flowers ore marigolds in May.
Thy floods are Ocean Seas, thy Towns to Provinces arise,
Whose civil government their wales has raisd to lofty skies.
Thy soil is fertill-temperate-sweete, no plague thine air does trouble,
Bastillyons four borne in thy bounds: two Seas and mountains double,
The Crocodile fierce-weeping-teares annoyeth not thy maine,
The speckled-race of crawling Serpents hant not thy domaine,
Not in one Acre of thy land that cursed seed is seen,
Backs-venimous-twinding to and fro t'infect thy meadows green.
The Tigre-swift of-foote prays not within thy mountains hollow,
Nor hungry-foming ore thy Plains enraged his chase does follow.
No Lions in thy deserts lurk: no Seahorse monster-rumbling,
Swimmes to thy maine, and steals thy infants under waves them tumbling.
If in thy streaming-riuers-rich-swift-gliding be not rold
The golden sands mong Pibble-stones, nor ore of massiue gold:
If silver pure dropping from down thy mountains be not found,
And every step Pearl, Ruby, Diamond, Grenate in thy ground,
In countre-change thou art as rich in Wadde, in Wool, in Wines,
In Salt Pits, Sindon fine, good Grain: which are sufficient mines
To make thee far and near renound of earthly Kingdoms Queens.
It Is only Peace, thou lackst, Alas! O God whose Eyes have seen
Our blessings and our miseries, thine Eyes of mercy glance
Upon our present State and quench the flames consuming France:
Sweet father turn our storms to calmes: Thy heavy hand retire,
Hide quickly in the Quiver all the Arrows of thine Ire.
FINIS.
